من
قوله : (قال الإمام الرازي في المباحث المشرقية إلخ) قيل : إن كانت هذه الكلمات الشيخ تعبيرات عن ما عنده، تبين أنه في حيرة من حقيقة العلم، لكن يحتمل أن يكون مراده بإيرادها الإشارة إلى اختلاف الآراء في تلك الحقيقة، ومختاره يكون واحداً منها وهذا أقرب مما نقل عن الشارح حيث قال : جاز أن يكون ابن سينا ذكر في تعريف العلم في كل موضع ما يحتاج
And perhaps they (the philosophers) made knowledge a privative thing, saying: it is the abstraction of the knower and the known from matter. And it was refuted by the necessity that every human person would then know all immaterial things, for the human soul is immaterial according to them. Closer than this is what was said: knowledge is the obtaining of a form abstracted from matter in an essence abstracted from it. There is no problem with the perceptions of the senses being excluded from the definition of knowledge, because the discussion concerns intellections, not sensations, as indicated by the previous discussions. Imam al-Razi said in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya: 'The speech of Ibn Sina on the reality of knowledge is confused, for he sometimes explains that...' (Text continues with glosses.) His saying: (And closer than this) i.e., than their saying that knowledge is the abstraction of the knower and the known because each of them is specific to intellection, except that the first is privative and the second existential, unlike what has passed that it is mental existence, for it includes the four types of knowledge. His saying: (The previous discussions in answer to the theologians' argument) including the distinction between identity and quiddity and that it is the intellectual form. His saying: (An error arising from homonymy) and it is possible that the source of error is imagining that quiddity in the first sense is the source for the occurrence of accidents absolutely, whether external or not. His saying: (For they said: it is the abstraction of the knower and the known) contains an apparent circle that can be repelled by what we mentioned in the definition of knowledge from the first chapter. His saying: (And it was refuted by the necessity, etc.) and it may be answered that what they meant is that knowledge is the relation that occurs between the knower and the known when they are abstracted, and it is not hidden that understanding that from the wording of the definition is far-fetched. Closer than it is to say: what is intended is their abstraction in the state of relation and addition, for without it the terms 'knower' and 'known' are not applied. His saying: (As indicated by the previous discussions) i.e., the discussions related to refuting mental existence brought by the theologians, and devoting the discussions of sensible things from the discussions of knowledge also indicates that. His saying: (Imam al-Razi said in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya, etc.) It is said: if these words of the Shaykh are expressions of what he holds, it shows he is in perplexity about the reality of knowledge, but it is possible that his aim in citing them is to indicate the divergence of opinions on that reality, and his chosen view would be one of them. This is closer than what is transmitted from the Sharh, where he said: 'It is possible that Ibn Sina mentioned in every place what is needed in defining knowledge.'
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage evaluates and refines philosophical definitions of knowledge. It begins by presenting the view of some philosophers that knowledge is a privation, i.e., the abstraction of both knower and known from matter. The commentator refutes this by noting that if the human soul is already immaterial, then all immaterial things would be known to every person—a clearly false consequence. A better definition is then offered: knowledge is the obtaining of an immaterial form in an immaterial essence. This avoids the privative issue and fits with the earlier discussion of mental existence. The commentator then explains that sense perceptions are excluded because the subject here is intellection, not sensation. The passage then becomes a detailed gloss on various points raised in the original text. It discusses the distinction between identity and quiddity, the source of error in equivocation, a potential circularity in the abstraction definition (which can be resolved by referring to the earlier definition in al-Mawaqif), and an interpretation of the abstraction definition as a relation between knower and known. Finally, the commentator transmits al-Razi's observation that Ibn Sina's statements on knowledge are confused, but notes that this could be because Ibn Sina was surveying different opinions rather than expressing his own confusion. The lesson thus refines the definition of knowledge by contrasting it with inadequate philosophical alternatives and highlighting epistemological foundations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge is simply the removal of matter from the knower and known.
Correction: Knowledge requires the positive presence of an immaterial form in an immaterial subject; mere abstraction leads to absurdity.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator reject the definition of knowledge as 'abstraction from matter'?
A) Because it implies the soul is material.
B) Because it would require every human to know all immaterial things automatically.
C) Because it contradicts the theologians' view of mental existence.
D) Because it is a privative definition and therefore invalid.

Key Takeaway
The correct definition of knowledge is not mere abstraction but the active presence of an immaterial form in an immaterial subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If the philosophers' mistaken definition were true, you would know all immaterial realities by virtue of your soul's nature—but you do not. Does this humbling fact drive you to pursue knowledge of the unseen with greater urgency?
?
The passage separates intellection from sensation. Are you content with a faith built only on sensory experiences and feelings, or do you strive to cultivate rational, intellectual conviction in the truths of aqeedah? What concrete step can you take today to move from sensation to intellection?

Critique of the Two Doubts: Equivocation on 'Mahiyya'
The commentator explains that the two doubts of the theologians stem from equivocation on the term 'mahiyya'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the theological doubt about knowledge of external objects arises from a linguistic equivocation on the term 'mahiyya'.

Core Idea
Equivocation on 'mahiyya' fuels the theologians' doubts about mental existence.

In this passage, the commentator identifies the root of a major theological confusion. The two doubts raised by theologians about how we can know external objects stem from an ambiguity in the word 'mahiyya'. Understanding this linguistic point clarifies the entire debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins mid-sentence, continuing from a previous discussion.
2
Identify the two meanings of 'mahiyya'
The commentator explains that 'mahiyya' can refer to the mental concept or the external object. Find where he states this.
3
Understand the error
The theologians mistakenly treated these two meanings as one thing, leading to false conclusions. See how the commentator describes this mistake.
The error is called 'ishtirak al-lafz' (equivocation).
4
Reflect on the resolution
Realizing that 'mahiyya' has two distinct referents dissolves the two doubts. Ask yourself how this resolves the problem of knowing external objects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والسماء) في ذهننا، فإنَّ هذه الهوية هي المتصفة بالعظم المانع من الحصول في أذهاننا، لا ماهيتهما ) إذ ليس فيها ما يمنع من حصولها فيها، (وهذا ) الذي ذكره المتكلمون في هاتين الشبهتين ( غلط واقع من جهة اشتراك اللفظ فإنَّ الماهية) أي لفظها ( تطلق على الأمر المعقول الذي هو الماهية الموجودة بالوجود الذهني ) وعلى ما يطابقه ) أي يطابق ذلك الأمر المعقول وهو الموجود الخارجي ( فظن أمراً واحداً، وبنى عليه اشتراكهما في الأحكام ،كلها وقد تبين لك فساد ذلك الظن.
And the sky in our mind, for this identity (huwiyya) is characterized by grandeur preventing its obtaining in our minds, not their essences (mahiyya) since there is nothing in them preventing their obtaining there. And this which the theologians mentioned in these two doubts is an error arising from equivocation of the word, for 'mahiyya' (i.e., its word) is applied to the intelligible thing which is the essence existing by mental existence, and to what corresponds to it, i.e., corresponds to that intelligible thing which is the external existent. So he supposed them one thing, and built upon that their sharing in all judgments, and the corruption of that supposition has become clear to you.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage is a critique of a theological argument. It begins by describing one of the two doubts: the sky (or something similar) in our mind—its identity (huwiyya) is characterized by a grandeur that prevents it from being actually present in our minds, whereas its essence (mahiyya) has no such barrier. This, the commentator says, is the basis of the theologians' doubt. Then he states that this error arises from equivocation (ishtirak al-lafz) in the word 'mahiyya'. The term 'mahiyya' can refer either to the intelligible concept that exists in the mind (the mental existence) or to the external object that corresponds to that concept. The theologians mistakenly treated these two meanings as one and the same, and then concluded that both share all the same properties. The commentator asserts that the falsity of this assumption has already been shown. Thus, the passage explains that the theological doubt is based on a linguistic confusion, not on a real difference in the nature of knowledge.

الوجود الذهني (mental existence)
الوجود الخارجي (external existence)
اشتراك اللفظ (equivocation)
الشبهتان (the two doubts)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the theologians' doubt is valid and points to a real problem in knowing external objects.
Correction: The passage shows the doubt stems from a semantic confusion: the term 'mahiyya' has two meanings (mental concept and external object), and the theologians mistakenly treated them as identical.

Checkpoint

B) The term 'mahiyya' is ambiguous, referring both to the mental concept and the external object
C) The sky cannot be in the mind due to its grandeur
D) The identity and essence of a thing are identical

Key Takeaway
The theologians' doubt about mental existence is resolved by recognizing that 'mahiyya' is ambiguous between mental concept and external object.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Take time to examine a key term like 'iman' or 'qadr'—could equivocation be causing you to misunderstand something about your faith?
?
The theologians' error came from assuming that because the word is the same, the realities are the same. Are there areas in your own thinking about Allah or the unseen where you might be slipping from the linguistic to the actual without realizing it? The Refutation of Knowledge as Mere Abstraction
Definition of knowledge as tajarrud, its refutation, and a better definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the definition of knowledge as abstraction (tajarrud) is rejected and what a more precise definition entails.

Core Idea
Knowledge is not mere abstraction; it is the attainment of an abstract form in an abstract essence.

Philosophers once defined knowledge as the abstraction of both the knower and the known from matter. At first glance, this seems plausible, but it leads to an absurd consequence. In this passage, the commentator refutes that definition and presents a refined alternative that becomes the foundation for subsequent discussions about intellections.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage once without overthinking. Then read the translation to get the full meaning. Notice the three parts: the definition, the refutation, and the better alternative.
2
Identify the problematic definition
The passage starts by stating that some philosophers made knowledge a 'non-existent thing' and defined it as abstraction from matter. What does 'non-existent' mean here? It means they considered knowledge a mere negation.
Think about why abstraction is considered a privative (adami) concept.
3
Understand the refutation
The commentator refutes this by showing a logical consequence: if knowledge is just abstraction, then every human soul—which is abstract according to these philosophers—would know all abstract things. That is clearly false, so the definition fails.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Study the alternative definition
The passage then offers a 'closer' definition: knowledge is the attainment of a form abstracted from matter in an essence also abstracted from matter. Notice that this adds the idea of 'attainment' (husul), making knowledge a positive relation rather than a mere negation.
The commentator also clarifies that sensory perceptions are excluded because the discussion is about intellections, not sensations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وربما جعلوه ) أي الحكماء العلم أمراً) عدمياً فقالوا : هو تجرد العالم والمعلوم من المادة ) ، ورد بأنه يلزم منه أن يكون كل شخص إنساني عالماً بجميع المجردات فإنَّ النفس الإنسانية مجردة عندهم، وأقرب من هذا ما قيل : إن العلم حصول صورة مجردة المادة عند ذات مجردة عنها، ولابأس بخروج إدراكات الحواس عن تعريف العلم، لأن الكلام في التعقلات دون الإحساسات كما دلت عليه المباحث السابقة.
And perhaps they—i.e., the philosophers—made knowledge a non-existent thing and said: it is the abstraction (tajarrud) of the knower and the known from matter. And it was refuted by the fact that it would entail that every human person knows all abstract things, for the human soul is abstract according to them. Closer than this is what was said: knowledge is the attainment of a form abstracted from matter in an essence abstracted from it. And there is no problem with excluding sensory perceptions from the definition of knowledge, because the discussion is about intellections, not sensations, as indicated by the previous investigations.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of definitions of knowledge (ilm). It first presents a definition by some philosophers who considered knowledge a privative (adami) thing: knowledge is the abstraction (tajarrud) of both knower and known from matter. The commentator immediately refutes this definition: if knowledge were simply abstraction, then every human being, whose soul is abstract according to these philosophers, would know all abstract things—which is false. Then the passage presents a better definition: knowledge is the attainment of a form that is abstracted from matter in an essence that is also abstracted from matter. This definition is 'closer' to the truth. The commentator also notes that sensory perceptions are excluded from this definition because the discussion concerns intellections (ta'aqqulat), not sensations (ihsasat), as earlier investigations have shown. Thus, the lesson refutes a flawed definition and endorses a refined one.

Key Terms
التجرد (tajarrud, abstraction)
العلم (knowledge)
الصورة المجردة (abstracted form)
التعقلات (intellections)
الإحساسات (sensations)
عدمي (non-existent, privative)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge is merely the abstraction (tajarrud) of the knower and the known from matter, a privative condition.
Correction: Knowledge is not a mere negation; it is a positive attainment of an abstract form in an abstract essence. The abstraction definition leads to absurd consequences, such as every human knowing all abstract things.

Checkpoint

C) Sensory perceptions would be excluded
D) The human soul is not abstract

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is not a mere privation of matter but a positive attainment of an abstract form in an abstract essence, and this definition excludes sensory perceptions which are not part of intellections.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Ibn Sina's Three Definitions of Knowledge: Al-Razi's Critique
Al-Razi cites Ibn Sina's three inconsistent definitions of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Ibn Sina gave three different definitions of knowledge in different contexts, revealing inconsistency in his epistemology.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina's 'consistent' idea of knowledge was actually three different ideas.

Al-Razi, a master of philosophical critique, exposes a fatal inconsistency in Ibn Sina's theory of knowledge. By examining three different contexts where Ibn Sina defines knowledge, al-Razi shows that the philosopher's account lacks coherence. Let's read the passage to see the three definitions and understand why this matters for Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation once to grasp al-Razi's claim of inconsistency. Notice the three distinct contexts he mentions.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the three definitions
List each definition: 1) abstraction from matter, 2) quality with relation, 3) presence of the form. Note the occasion for each.
Write them down to see the differences clearly.
3
Understand al-Razi's critique
Ask yourself: why does al-Razi consider these definitions incompatible? Think about what each definition implies about the nature of knowledge and how they conflict.
Consider the problem of multiplicity in the divine essence.
4
Reflect on the broader lesson
This critique shows the difficulty of defining knowledge in philosophy. In kalam, definitions must be consistent with tawhid and reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الإمام الرازي في المباحث المشرقية : قد اضطرب كلام ابن سينا في حقيقة العلم فحيث بين أن كون الباري عقلاً وعاقلاً ومعقولاً لا يقتضي كثرة في ذاته، فسر العلم بالتجرد عن المادة، وحيث قيد اندراج العلم في مقولة الكيف بالذات، وفي مقولة المضاف بالعرض، جعله عبارة عن صفة ذات إضافة، وحيث ذكر أن تعقل الشيء لذاته ولغير ذاته ليس إلا حضور صورته عنده جعله عبارة عن الصورة المرتسمة في الجوهر العاقل.
Imam al-Razi said in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya: 'The speech of Ibn Sina has become confused regarding the reality of knowledge. For where he explained that the Necessary Existent being intellect, intelligent, and intelligible does not necessitate multiplicity in His essence, he interpreted knowledge as abstraction from matter. And where he restricted the inclusion of knowledge in the category of quality essentially and in the category of relation accidentally, he made it a description of a quality with relation. And where he mentioned that intellection of a thing for itself and for other than itself is nothing but the presence of its form with it, he made it a description of the form impressed in the intelligent substance.'
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage presents a citation from Imam al-Razi's al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya, in which he critiques Ibn Sina for inconsistency in defining knowledge. Al-Razi lists three distinct definitions given by Ibn Sina in different contexts: first, when discussing the Necessary Existent being 'aql, 'aqil, and ma'qul without multiplicity, Ibn Sina defined knowledge as abstraction from matter (tajarrud 'an al-madda). Second, when discussing the categories, Ibn Sina classified knowledge as a quality (kayf) essentially and as a relative (mudaf) accidentally, thus defining it as a quality with relation (sifa dhat idafa). Third, when discussing intellection, he defined knowledge as the presence of the form (hudur suratihi) of the object, i.e., the form impressed in the intelligent substance (al-sura al-murtasima fi al-jawhar al-'aqil). Al-Razi uses these three definitions to show that Ibn Sina's account of the reality of knowledge is unsettled and contradictory. The lesson thus highlights the lack of consensus among philosophers on the nature of knowledge and serves as a caution against overconfident metaphysical claims.

Key Terms
العلم
التجرد عن المادة
صفة ذات إضافة
الصورة المرتسمة
الجوهر العاقل
مقولة الكيف
مقولة المضاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibn Sina had a single, consistent definition of knowledge.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that Ibn Sina gave at least three different definitions depending on the context, indicating inconsistency.

Checkpoint

B) Two

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's definitions of knowledge varied by context, undermining a unified philosophical account.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Tajarrud as a Condition for Intellection, Not the Definition
Commentary distinguishes condition from definition via Ibn Sina's text.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why abstraction (tajarrud) is considered a necessary condition for intellection rather than the definition of knowledge, and how this distinction preserves the proper understanding of divine attributes.

Core Idea
Abstraction is the door to knowledge, not knowledge itself.

This passage from the commentary on al-Mawaqif addresses a subtle but crucial point: when Ibn Sina defines knowledge as abstraction from matter, he is not giving a definition of knowledge, but identifying a necessary condition. The commentator draws on a long quotation from al-Shifa to show that abstraction (tajarrud) is what makes intellection possible, not what intellection is. By carefully distinguishing between condition and definition, the commentator clarifies a common misinterpretation and safeguards the transcendence of divine knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation carefully
Start by reading the full Arabic passage and its English translation. Let the words of Ibn Sina and the commentator sink in before analyzing.
Pay attention to the technical terms: tajarrud, 'aql, ma'qul.
2
Identify the key distinction
The commentator's main point is that abstraction is a condition (shart) for intellection, not the definition ('ayn al-'ilm) itself. Find where he states this explicitly.
Look for the phrase 'the speech proclaims with the loudest voice that abstraction is a condition for intellection'.
3
Analyze Ibn Sina's argument
In the quotation, Ibn Sina explains why the Necessary Existent is pure intellect and pure intelligible: because He is free from matter and its attachments, which are the obstacles to intellection. Note how matter is presented as the obstacle, not the presence or absence of abstraction as the essence.
Ibn Sina says: 'the cause of a thing not being intellected is matter and its attachments, not its existence.'
4
Reflect on the theological implications
If abstraction were the definition of knowledge, then God's knowledge would be the same as His abstraction, which could imply multiplicity. The commentator shows that abstraction is merely the removal of obstacles, making intellection possible without equating to it.
This protects divine simplicity and transcendence.
5
Summarize the lesson
Write a one-sentence summary of the lesson in your own words, emphasizing the condition vs. definition distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فسر العلم بالتجرد عن (المادة في الشفاء الواجب لذاته عقل محض، لأنه ذات مفارقة عن المادة من كل وجه، وقد عرفت أن السبب في أن لا يعقل الشيء هو المادة وعلائقها لا وجوده وأما الوجود الصوري فهو الوجود العقلي وهو الوجود الذي إذا تقرر في شيء صار للشيء به عقل والذي يحتمل بنيله هو عقل بالقوة والذي ناله بالفعل هو عقل بالفعل على سبيل الاستكمال، والذي هو له ذاته هو عقل بذاته، وكذلك هو معقول محض، لأن المانع للشيء أن يكون معقولاً هو أن يكون في مادة وعلائقها، وهو المانع من أن يكون عقلاً. وقد تبين لك هذا المادة والعلائق المتحقق الوجود المفارق وهو معقول لذاته ولأنه عقل بذاته وهو أيضاً معقول بذاته، فهو معقول ذاته فذاته عقل ومعقول وعاقل لا أن هناك أشياء متكثرة، وذلك لأنه بما هو هوية مجردة عقل، وبما يعتبر له أن هويته المجردة لذاته فهو معقول لذاته . وبما يعتبر له أنَّ ذاته له هوية مجردة هو عاقل ذاته انتهى. ولا يخفى على المنصف أنه كلام ينادي بأعلى صوته بأن التجرد شرط للتعقل، وأن المادة ولواحقها مانع له وأنّ العقل هو الهوية المجردة .
His saying: 'He interpreted knowledge as abstraction from matter in al-Shifa: The Necessary Existent in His essence is pure intellect, because He is a substance separate from matter in every respect. And you have known that the cause of a thing not being intellected is matter and its attachments, not its existence. As for formal existence, it is intellectual existence, and it is the existence which, if it is established in a thing, the thing becomes intellect by it. That which can attain it is intellect in potentiality, and that which has attained it actually is intellect in actuality by way of completion. And that for which its essence is that is intellect by itself. Likewise, He is pure intelligible, because the obstacle to a thing being intelligible is that it be in matter and its attachments, and that is the obstacle to it being intellect. And this has been made clear to you: matter and attachments, the separate existence that is intelligible in itself and because it is intellect by itself, and it is also intelligible by itself. So He is the intelligible of His essence, so His essence is intellect, intelligible, and intelligent, not that there are many things. That is because insofar as it is an abstract identity (huwiyya mujarrada) it is intellect, and insofar as it is considered that its abstract identity is for itself, it is intelligible of itself, and insofar as it is considered that its essence has an abstract identity for itself, it is intelligent of its essence.' End. And it is not hidden from the fair-minded that this speech proclaims with the loudest voice that abstraction (tajarrud) is a condition for intellection, and that matter and its attachments are an obstacle to it, and that the intellect is the abstract identity.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage is an extended explanation and quotation from Ibn Sina's al-Shifa, focusing on the nature of the Necessary Existent and the role of abstraction. The commentator begins by glossing the phrase 'He interpreted knowledge as abstraction from matter in al-Shifa.' The quoted text from Ibn Sina argues that the Necessary Existent is pure intellect because it is completely separate from matter. The cause of something not being intellected is matter and its attachments, not its existence. Formal existence (al-wujud al-suri) is intellectual existence. A thing that can attain this existence is intellect in potentiality; when it actually attains it, it is intellect in actuality. The Necessary Existent, whose essence is that, is intellect by itself. Similarly, He is pure intelligible, because the obstacle to being intelligible is matter. He is intelligible of His essence because His essence is intellect, intelligible, and intelligent—without multiplicity. Then the commentator draws the lesson: this passage makes it clear that abstraction (tajarrud) is not the definition of knowledge but a necessary condition for intellection, and matter and its attachments are the obstacle. The intellect (al-'aql) is the abstract identity (al-huwiyya al-mujarrada). Thus, the passage clarifies that Ibn Sina's text, when read carefully, does not define knowledge as mere abstraction but as something that requires abstraction as a precondition.

Key Terms
التجرد (tajarrud)
العقل (intellect)
المعقول (intelligible)
العاقل (intelligent)
الهوية المجردة (abstract identity)
الوجود الصوري (formal existence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Ibn Sina's statement 'knowledge is abstraction from matter' means that abstraction is the definition of knowledge.
Correction: The commentator explicitly states that the passage shows abstraction is a condition (shart) for intellection, not the definition ('ayn al-'ilm) itself. Matter and its attachments are the obstacles; removing them through abstraction enables intellection, but abstraction is not what knowledge essentially is.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what role does abstraction (tajarrud) play in knowledge?
A) It is the definition of knowledge
B) It is a condition for intellection
C) It is the essence of the intellect
D) It is an obstacle to knowledge

Key Takeaway
Abstraction (tajarrud) is a necessary condition for intellection, but it is not the definition of knowledge itself; matter and its attachments are the obstacles that abstraction removes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that matter and its attachments are obstacles to intellection. What specific attachment (a desire, a possession, a relationship, a habit) in your life is clouding your mind from knowing the truth about Allah? What will you do this week to begin detaching from it? Be concrete.

?

Do you think of His attributes as separate parts? Commit to one specific change in the way you think about tawhid starting today.

Definition of Knowledge and Objection Regarding Knowledge by Aspect
Commentary critiques definition of knowledge as correspondence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the definition of knowledge as 'correspondence to the essence of the intelligible' fails when knowledge is by an aspect rather than the essence.

Core Idea
Knowledge by aspect does not correspond to the full essence.

The commentator challenges a classic definition of knowledge. If we know something only through one of its attributes, what is imprinted in the mind is not the entire essence but that attribute. How then can we speak of correspondence? This lesson examines the objection and a possible defense.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Understand the problem: the definition of knowledge as correspondence to the essence of the intelligible does not appear true when knowledge is by an aspect.
This is the core criticism.
2
Examine the example
Consider the case of knowing a human being through the attribute 'the laughing one'. The essence is humanity, but what is imprinted is only the concept of laughter.
The imprinted form does not correspond to the entire essence.
3
Consider the saving interpretation
The commentator mentions a possible defense: if 'correspondence' means merely that the mental form applies to individuals, then the objection does not arise.
4
Reflect on the commentator's final remark
The commentator notes that this interpretation is far-fetched and that a better definition would be 'acquisition of an essence perceived in the immaterial substance'.
This shows the definition's inadequacy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( المطابقة لماهية المعقول هذا التعريف لا يظهر صدقه فيما إذا علم الشيء لا بكنهه، بل بوجه من وجوهه كما يعلم الإنسان بالضاحك، فإن المعقول هاهنا هو نفس الماهية الإنسانية، وأما المرتسم في الجوهر العاقل فهو وجهه أعني مفهوم الضاحك اللهم إلا أن يراد بالمطابقة مجرد صدق المطابق على إفراد المطابق فحينئذ لا يرد عليه ما ذكر، نعم يرد على تعريف العلم بحصول ماهية تدرك في الذات المجردة، إلا أن فهم هذا المعنى من المطابقة بعيد.
His statement: 'Correspondence to the essence of the intelligible' – this definition does not appear true when something is known not by its essence but by one of its aspects, as man is known by 'the laughing one'. For the intelligible here is the human essence itself, but what is imprinted in the intellectual substance is its aspect, namely the concept of 'the laughing one'. Unless what is meant by correspondence is merely the truth of the correspondent [predicate] of the individuals of the correspondent [subject]; then what was mentioned does not apply. Yes, it applies to the definition of knowledge as the acquisition of an essence perceived in the immaterial substance, but understanding this meaning from 'correspondence' is unlikely.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage is a criticism of a definition of knowledge (ʿilm) as 'correspondence (muṭābaqah) to the essence of the intelligible (māhiyyat al-maʿqūl)'. The commentator (al-Jurjānī) points out that this definition fails when we know something not by its essence but by one of its attributes (wajh). For example, when we know a human being through the attribute 'the laughing one' (al-ḍāḥik), the intelligible is the human essence itself, but what is actually imprinted in the intellect is only the aspect, i.e., the concept of laughter. Thus the imprinted form does not correspond to the entire essence. The commentator then offers a saving interpretation: if by 'correspondence' one means merely that the mental form applies to the individuals of the extramental reality (i.e., it is true of them), then the objection does not arise. However, he notes that this understanding is far-fetched, and a better definition would be 'the acquisition of an essence perceived in the immaterial substance', though that too is not captured by the literal meaning of correspondence.

Key Terms
علم
مطابقة
ماهية المعقول
الوجه
المرتسم
الجوهر العاقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Definition of knowledge as correspondence works for all cases of knowledge.
Correction: It fails when knowledge is by an aspect rather than the essence, as shown by the example of knowing man through laughter.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator reject the definition of knowledge as 'correspondence to the essence of the intelligible'?
A) Because correspondence is impossible for immaterial forms.
B) Because it does not apply when we know something by one of its aspects, not its essence.
C) Because the mind cannot form images of essences.
D) Because the definition only works for God's knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The definition of knowledge as correspondence is inadequate for knowledge by aspect; a more precise definition is needed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your Lord, do you realize that all your knowledge of Him is through His attributes, not His essence? How does that humbling realization change the way you speak about Allah? Commit today to repent whenever you feel you have described Allah beyond what His attributes allow.
?
You often pride yourself on 'knowing' someone based on a single trait or action. But this lesson shows that knowing by an aspect is not knowing the essence. Is there a person in your life you have reduced to a label? What would it cost you to seek their true essence instead? Can you make a concrete step this week to see them more fully?
?
The saving interpretation of 'correspondence' as merely predication was deemed unlikely. Why do you settle for superficial, 'true' statements about your faith (e.g., 'I believe in Allah') without penetrating to the essence? What is one area of your deen where you have settled for aspect-knowledge, and what will you do today to go deeper?

Simple and Detailed Intellection
Defines and distinguishes simple and detailed intellection with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between simple and detailed intellection, and how the example from our own knowledge illustrates the relation between them.

Core Idea
Simple knowledge is a single whole that produces detail when expressed.

This passage lays out two fundamental ways of knowing: one that sees all at once (simple) and one that unfolds step by step (detailed). The author uses an example from our own experience to show how they relate. As you read, notice how the simple act of knowing something precedes its detailed expression.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
Read the passage and locate where the author defines 'simple' and 'detailed' intellection. Note the key terms in Arabic.
Simple intellection is associated with separate beings; detailed with the soul.
2
Examine the example from al-Mulakhkhas
Consider the example of knowing the sky and earth in a simple way versus explaining it in detail. Why does the author say the simple one is a principle of the detailed?
This example is from our own necessary knowledge.
3
Reflect on the relation
Think about how simple knowledge serves as the source from which detailed knowledge emerges. How does this relate to the idea of productive knowledge?
The Sheikh's explanation of simple knowledge as 'one that emanates forms' is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثاني - المقصد الأول : إضافة ما بين العالم والمعلوم. قوله: (للصور المفصلة في النفس التعقل التفصيلي لا يكون إلا في النفس وتعقل المفارقات تعقل بسيط. قال الشيخ في كتاب النفس: العلم البسيط الذي ليس من شأنه أن يكون في نفسه صورة بعد صورة لكن هو واحد يفيض عنه الصور في قابل الصور فذلك علم فاعل للشيء الذي نسميه علماً ومبدأ له وذلك هو القوة العقلية المطلقة من النفس المشاكلة للعقول الفعالة وأما التفصيل فهو للنفس من حيث هو نفس، فما لم يكن له ذلك لم يكن له علم نفساني إلى أن قال: وعلى هذا ينبغي أن يعتقد الحال في المفارقات المحضة في عقلها الأشياء فإن عقلها هو العقل الفعال للصور الخلاق لها. قال في الملخص: إنا نعلم بالضرورة علمنا بالسماء والأرض ووجودنا ووجود لذاتنا وآلامنا، ونميز بينه وبين حاصلاً بالفعل التام لا على سبيل التفصيل بل على الوجه البسيط وهذا كمن كان عالماً بمسألة ثم سئل عنها فإنه يستحضر الجواب في ذهنه دفعة واحدة لكن لا على سبيل التفصيل فإن التفصيل إنما يحصل عند شروعه في بيان ذلك وهذا النوع الثالث مبدأ للنوع الثاني وتعقل الواجب والمفارقات من هذا القبيل إلا أن العقل البسيط للنفس مغاير لذاتها، وفي الواجب والمجردات عينها. وخلاصة ما في كتاب المباحثات العقل البسيط في الأول هو ذاته بخلاف العقل البسيط الذي يحصل فينا وكما يلزم العقل البسيط الذي يحصل لنا من المعقولات المفصلة فكذلك يلزم العقل البسيط في الأول الذي هو ذاته ذات لوازمه التي هي المعقولات المفصلة.
The second type - the first section: The relation between the knower and the known. His saying: (Concerning detailed forms in the soul: detailed intellection is only in the soul, and the intellection of the separate beings is simple intellection. The Sheikh said in the Book of the Soul: Simple knowledge that is not apt to have in itself form after form, but it is one from which forms emanate upon receptive matter; that is productive knowledge of the thing that we call knowledge and a principle of it, and that is the absolute rational power of the soul that resembles the active intellects. As for detailed intellection, it belongs to the soul insofar as it is a soul; so if it does not have that, it has no psychic knowledge, until he said: And on this one should believe the situation of pure separate beings in their intellection of things, for their intellection is the active intellect of the forms that produce them. It is said in al-Mulakhkhas: We know necessarily our knowledge of the sky and the earth, our existence, the existence of our pleasures and pains, and we distinguish between it and what is actualized by complete actualization, not in a detailed manner but in a simple manner; this is like someone who knows a matter and then is asked about it, for he brings the answer to mind all at once, but not in a detailed manner; for detail only occurs when he begins to explain it; this third type is a principle of the second type; and the intellection of the Necessary and the separate beings is of this kind; except that the simple intellect of the soul is other than its essence, whereas in the Necessary and the simple separate beings it is identical to them. And the summary of what is in Kitab al-Mubahathat: The simple intellect in the First is His essence, unlike the simple intellect that occurs in us; and just as the simple intellect that occurs in us is necessarily accompanied by detailed intelligibles, so too the simple intellect in the First, which is His essence, is necessarily accompanied by the essences of His concomitants, which are the detailed intelligibles.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage defines and distinguishes two types of intellection: detailed (tafsili) and simple (basit). It begins by citing the original text (قوله) that detailed intellection exists only in the soul, while intellection of separate immaterial beings (mufariqat) is simple. The Sheikh in the Book of the Soul explains simple knowledge as a single unity that emanates forms to receptive matter, making it productive (fa'il) and a principle of knowledge. Detailed knowledge is psychological, requiring a soul. The example from al-Mulakhkhas illustrates simple knowledge: one knows a topic fully but only recalls it in a simple, undetailed manner until explaining. This third type (simple intellection) is the principle of the second (detailed). Importantly, for the Necessary Being and other immaterial beings, the simple intellect is identical to their essence, unlike in us where it is distinct. The essence of the First, being simple, necessarily includes its concomitants (which are the detailed intelligibles of all things).

Key Terms
تعقل بسيط
تعقل تفصيلي
نفس
مفارقات
عقل فاعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Simple knowledge means having no content or being vague.
Correction: Simple knowledge is actually a complete but undivided awareness that contains all detail implicitly, as shown by the example of knowing something fully before expressing it.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, where does detailed intellection occur?
A) In the soul
B) In the active intellect
C) In separate beings
D) In the Necessary Being

Key Takeaway
Simple intellection is the undivided source from which detailed knowledge unfolds.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own existence or the sky above you, do you experience it as a simple, immediate awareness, or do you automatically break it into details? How often do you pause to dwell on that simple, unified awareness before analyzing it? What would change if you sat with that simple awareness for a few minutes each day?
?
The passage says simple knowledge is productive—it is a principle that gives rise to detailed knowledge. In your own life, what simple truths (e.g., 'God exists', 'I am not my own creator') have become productive foundations for your detailed beliefs and actions? Are those foundations solid, or have you built on shifting sand?
?
Imagine someone asks you a deep question about your faith or your life’s purpose. Does an answer come to your mind all at once, simply and clearly? Or does it feel scattered and detailed without unity? What does your immediate response reveal about whether your knowledge is truly simple and certain? Are you willing to let that simple answer guide your detailed explanations?

Divine Simple Intellection and Its Distinction
Distinguishes divine simple intellection from human simple intellection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how simple intellection in God is identical to His essence, unlike in humans, and why that matters for divine knowledge.

Core Idea
In God, knowing and being are one; in us, they are separate.

Having established the two types of intellection, the passage now turns to a crucial difference: simple intellection in the Necessary Being and separate beings is not distinct from their essence, whereas in us it is. This section clarifies how divine knowledge is active and self-sufficient, contrasting with human receptive knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Locate the key distinction
Find where the passage says the simple intellect of the soul is other than its essence, while in the Necessary and separate beings it is identical.
This is the central point of this part.
2
Understand the concomitance
Read the summary from al-Mubahathat: just as our simple intellect is accompanied by detailed intelligibles, so God's simple essence is accompanied by His concomitants (the detailed intelligibles of all things).
This shows how divine knowledge encompasses all detail without multiplicity in essence.
3
Reflect on implications
Think about why this distinction matters for the nature of God and for creation. How does God's knowledge being His essence make it uncaused and perfect?
This is a key difference between created and uncreated intellects.

What Is Kalam?
beginner

The Self-Evident Conception of Knowledge
Argument and response on self-evidence of knowledge conception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the conception of knowledge is considered self-evident in kalam reasoning and how it defends against an objection about partial versus absolute knowledge.

Core Idea
The conception of knowledge is self-evident because it underlies all psychological states.

In this passage, the author asserts that the conception of knowledge is self-evident. He argues that since all psychological states depend on knowing what knowledge is, that foundational conception must itself be self-evident. An objector then challenges that only particular knowledge is conceived, not absolute knowledge. The author answers that a partial conception suffices for self-evidence. This lesson unpacks that argument and its defense.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation side by side. Notice the concise style and the flow from claim to objection to answer.
The passage uses logical terms like 'necessary' and 'self-evident'.
2
Identify the main claim
The passage begins with a claim: the conception of knowledge is self-evident. Identify the premise that supports this claim — what is the reasoning?
The key phrase is 'ما يتوقف عليه البديهي أولى أن يكون بديهياً'.
3
Analyze the objection
The objector argues that only particular knowledge (juz'i) is necessarily conceived, not absolute knowledge (mutlaq). Understand why this objection threatens the initial claim.
The objection is introduced by 'قوله : ( وذلك يتوقف إلخ)'.
4
Understand the answer
The author responds by clarifying that the purpose is conception of knowledge 'in some respect', not in its full absolute sense. That is sufficient for self-evidence.
The answer uses 'بوجه ما' (in some respect).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سائر الأحوال النفسانية، وذلك يتوقف على تصور ماهية العلم، وما يتوقف عليه البديهي أولى أن يكون بديهياً، فتصور العلم بديهي. قوله : ( وذلك يتوقف إلخ) فيه أن اللازم منه تصور العلم الجزئي بوجه ما بالضرورة ولا يلزم منه تصور العلم المطلق فضلاً عن أن يكون بديهياً والجواب أن المقصود تصوره بوجه ما وذلك لازم من تصور العلم الجزئي.
All psychological states depend on the conception of the essence of knowledge, and what the self-evident depends on is more deserving of being self-evident, so the conception of knowledge is self-evident. He said: (and that depends, etc.) In it [the objection] is that what follows from it is necessarily the conception of particular knowledge in some respect, and the conception of absolute knowledge does not follow from it, let alone that it be self-evident. The answer is that the purpose is its conception in some respect, and that follows from the conception of particular knowledge.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage addresses the claim that the conception (tasawwur) of knowledge is self-evident (badihi). The author begins by stating that all psychological states depend on the conception of the essence (mahiyya) of knowledge, and since the self-evident is more deserving of being self-evident, the conception of knowledge must itself be self-evident. An objector then argues that what is necessary is only the conception of particular knowledge (juz'i) in some respect, not absolute knowledge (mutlaq), so it cannot be claimed that the conception of knowledge is self-evident. The author answers that the intended conception is indeed in some respect, and that is sufficient; it follows from the conception of particular knowledge, so the claim stands.

Key Terms
تصور العلم
بديهي
العلم الجزئي
العلم المطلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'self-evident' requires a full and absolute conception of knowledge.
Correction: The passage clarifies that a conception 'in some respect' (بوجه ما) is sufficient for self-evidence; absolute detail is not required.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the conception of knowledge considered self-evident?
A) Because knowledge is a psychological state.
B) Because what the self-evident depends on is itself self-evident.
C) Because the objector conceded the point.
D) Because absolute knowledge is self-evident.

Key Takeaway
The conception of knowledge is self-evident because it is the foundation on which all other psychological states depend, and a partial conception suffices for self-evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to 'know' your beliefs, but have you ever paused to ask yourself what it truly means to know something? This lesson says the basic concept is self-evident, yet we often live on assumptions. In what area of your faith are you demanding absolute clarity before you act, when a partial understanding is sufficient? Today, identify one truth about Allah that you are certain of—even if you cannot define it fully—and let that move you to action.
?
The objection says the argument only gives a partial conception. The answer says that partial suffices. In your journey to know Allah, do you insist on exhaustive knowledge before obeying, or do you accept that a partial yet firm conception is enough to worship Him with sincerity? What would change in your daily practice if you stopped waiting for complete understanding and started acting on the self-evident truths you already possess?
?
All psychological states—your desires, fears, hopes—depend on the conception of knowledge. Is your emotional state today built on a clear foundation of knowing, or is it driven by ignorance and confusion? Commit to examining one belief that underlies a recurring emotion, and ask whether that belief is based on a self-evident truth or on a shaky assumption.

What Is Kalam?
intermediate

Knowledge Is a Positive Attribute: Refuting the Negation View
The author refutes the view that knowledge is a mere negation and argues it is a positive attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why knowledge cannot be defined as a mere negation or abstraction, and why it must be a positive attribute.

Core Idea
Knowledge is not the absence of ignorance—it is a real positive quality.

This passage tackles a fundamental question: Is knowledge simply the lack of ignorance? The author systematically shows that defining knowledge as a negation leads to contradictions, and even the idea of abstraction fails. Through careful analysis, he demonstrates that knowledge must be a positive attribute, either real or relational.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to get the overall flow of the argument.
Notice the structure: refutations followed by conclusion.
2
Understand the refutation of 'adam al-'adam
The author shows that if knowledge is a negation of negation, it becomes a positive concept, contradicting the assumption that it is a mere negation.
This is a self-defeating definition.
3
Analyze the objection of abstraction
Some might say knowledge is abstraction from matter, but the author gives two arguments: abstraction can exist without knowledge, and knowledge is relative while abstraction is not.
This refutes the alternative negative definition.
4
Grasp the conclusion
After refuting all negative definitions, the author concludes that knowledge must be a positive attribute, either real or relational.
This is the main takeaway.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيكون العلم عدماً للعدم، فيكون ثبوتياً مع فرض كونه عدمياً، وإما الجهل المركب وهو باطل أيضاً لخلو المحل عنهما معاً كما في الجماد، لا يقال : جاز أن يكون عبارة عن التجرد عن المادة، لأنا نقول: قد يعقل كون الشيء مجرداً، وهو أن لا يكون جسماً ولا جسمانياً مع الشك في كونه عالماً، وأيضاً يصح أن يقال في الشيء أنه عالم بهذا دون ذاك، ولا يصح أن يقال : إنه مجرد عن المادة بالنسبة إلى أحدهما دون الآخر، وإذا لم تكن تلك الحالة عدمية، فهي وجودية إما حقيقية أو إضافية. قوله : ( فيكون ثبوتياً) أي مفهوماً وجودياً لأن عدم العدم ثبوت بحسب الصدق، وإن كانا متغايرين بحسب المفهوم. قوله : ( لخلو المحل عنهما هذا إنما يتم لو كان عدماً للجهل المركب بمعنى وأما لو كان عدماً له بمعنى عدم الملكة فخلو المحل عنهما لا يضر. قوله : (لا يقال : جاز إلخ) منع للملازمة المستفادة من قوله: لو كانت عدماً لكانت ما يقابلها . عدم قوله : (فهي وجودية أي ليس السلب داخلاً فيها.
So knowledge would be a negation of negation, thus it would be positive while assuming it to be negative. As for compound ignorance, it is also invalid because the locus is devoid of both, as in inanimate objects. It cannot be said: perhaps it is an expression of abstraction from matter, because we say: a thing may be conceived as abstract, meaning that it is not a body nor a corporeal entity while doubting whether it is knowing. Also, it is correct to say of a thing that it knows this but not that, but it is not correct to say that it is abstract from matter with respect to one but not the other. And if that state is not negative, then it is positive, either real or relational. His statement: (thus it would be positive) meaning it is a positive concept because the negation of negation is positive in terms of truth, even if they differ in concept. His statement: (the locus is devoid of both) this is valid only if it were a negation of compound ignorance in one sense; but if it were a negation of it in the sense of lack of ability, then the locus being devoid of both does not harm. His statement: (it cannot be said: perhaps etc.) is a denial of the implication derived from his statement: if it were a negation, it would be its opposite. His statement: (so it is positive) meaning that negation is not included in it.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage refutes the view that knowledge is a mere negation. The author begins by considering the possibility that knowledge is 'adam al-'adam (negation of negation), which would result in a positive concept despite being defined negatively. He then examines compound ignorance (al-jahl al-murakkab) and dismisses it because the locus (the inanimate object) lacks both knowledge and its opposite. Next, he rejects the idea that knowledge is abstraction from matter, offering two arguments: first, a thing can be conceived as abstract without being knowing; second, knowledge is relative (a thing can know one thing but not another), whereas abstraction is not relative. Finally, he concludes that the state must be positive either really or relatively. The commentary then explains each point: the first comment clarifies that 'adam al-'adam is existentially positive; the second comment notes a nuance about the definition of compound ignorance; the third comment explains that the rebuttal of the abstraction view prevents an implication; the fourth comment asserts that positivity excludes negation from its concept.

وجودي إضافي

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge is merely the absence of ignorance or some kind of negation.
Correction: The passage shows that even if knowledge is defined as negation of negation, it becomes positive; other negative definitions like compound ignorance or abstraction also fail. Therefore, knowledge is a positive attribute.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude about the state of knowledge after refuting the negation and abstraction views?
A) It is a mere negation.
B) It is a positive attribute, either real or relational.
C) It is abstraction from matter.
D) It is compound ignorance.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is a positive attribute; it cannot be reduced to negation or abstraction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does your view affect your seriousness in seeking knowledge? This lesson challenges you to see knowledge not as default, but as something you must earn. Will you settle for being 'not ignorant' or will you strive to be truly knowledgeable?
?
The author refutes compound ignorance—being confidently wrong. Have you ever held a belief with certainty that later turned out false? That is compound ignorance. This lesson shows it is a real state, not just a lack of knowledge. Take a moment: what belief do you hold most strongly that might be based on assumption rather than evidence? What would it cost you to examine it?
?
If knowledge is positive and relational, then true knowledge distinguishes between what you know and what you don't. Do you know the limits of your own knowledge? The author shows that abstraction from matter does not equal knowledge. Are you content with vague, abstract concepts about Allah, or do you press for real, specific knowledge that affects your worship and daily life? Where do you need to move from abstraction to actual understanding?

The Form View and Its Refutation
The author examines and refutes the form view of knowledge, then affirms the relational view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why knowledge cannot be merely the form of the known, and why the relational view is necessary.

Core Idea
Knowledge is not just having the form; it requires a specific relation to an abstract self.

This passage delves into the nature of knowledge. The author considers three possibilities: knowledge as the form equal to the essence, as something else, or as a relation. He refutes the first by pointing out that inanimate objects possess forms without knowledge. This leads to the conclusion that knowledge is a relational attribute requiring consciousness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall argument without overanalyzing.
Notice the author's style of presenting and refuting views.
2
Understand the form view
Identify the first possibility: knowledge is the form equal to the essence of the perceived object.
3
Examine the refutation
The author uses the example of blackness present in an inanimate object. Ask yourself: why does this disprove the form view?
The inanimate object has the form but no knowledge.
4
Consider the relational view
The author affirms that knowledge (consciousness) involves a specific relation between subject and object. Note why he thinks this is obvious.
He says: 'we know necessarily that consciousness does not occur except with a specific relation.'
5
Reflect on the implications
Think about why this distinction matters for understanding divine attributes. How does the relational view preserve divine simplicity?
This is a foundational move in kalam.

As for the real, either it is the form equal to the essence of the perceived, which is false because the essence of blackness is present in an inanimate object yet there is no knowledge there. If it is answered that knowledge is not merely the obtaining of the essence of a thing for another, but it is a specific obtaining, namely the obtaining of the essence of the perceived for an abstract self, and the inanimate object is not an abstract self, we say: this is an admission that knowledge is not the obtaining itself. Or it is something else different from the form, and that has no evidence, though a group have said it. As for the relational, there is no doubt about its reality, for we know necessarily that consciousness does not occur except with a specific relation between the conscious subject and the object of consciousness. As for whether in the realization of this relation called consciousness another real, relational, or negative matter is considered, that is not needed in the investigation of the essence of knowledge. This is what is summarized from his speech, and it is not hidden from you what is in it. His statement: (because the essence of blackness etc.) therein that what obtains for the inanimate is the identity of blackness not its essence, and if granted, knowledge is not the essence itself but the form equal to it. His statement: (this) is an admission that knowledge is not the obtaining itself. His statement: (thus it would be positive) has been previously refuted in the discussion of opposition. His statement: (the locus is devoid of both) would require that blindness, for example, is not a negation, because if it were a negation of its opposite, sight, it would not be exempt from the locus being devoid of both, as in an inanimate object. In summary, it is permissible for the locus to be devoid of two opposites in the opposition of privation and possession, but not in the opposition of negation and affirmation. His statement: (and if that state is not negative, then it is positive) it is said: the necessary consequence of what he mentioned, assuming its completion, is that the mentioned state is not a negation, not that it is not negative, so it does not entail that it is positive. This only applies if by 'positive' he meant the existent. But if he meant that which does not have negation as part of its concept, then not. His statement: (because the essence of blackness obtains for the inanimate) the answer is that knowledge is the form obtained for a thing by a shadowy existence, while the essence of blackness obtains for the inanimate by a primary existence. If you say: this is an admission that knowledge is not... His statement: (and that has no evidence) the absence of proof does not indicate its non-existence in itself, but only indicates lack of knowledge of it, unless one argues that what has no proof must be negated, and you have known its weakness. His statement: (for we know necessarily that consciousness does not occur except with a specific relation) the apparent wording here suggests that consciousness is different from that relation and occurs with it, but he has explicitly stated that that relation is consciousness itself. So perhaps he first built the speech on a conceptual difference, or he meant by consciousness first the verbal meaning and second the technical meaning, and the second is not free from artificiality.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage examines the real existential attribute. The author first considers the possibility that knowledge is the form equal to the essence of the perceived object. He refutes it by noting that the essence of blackness is present in an inanimate object, yet the inanimate object does not have knowledge. If the opponent responds that knowledge is a specific obtaining (for an abstract self), the author says this is an admission that knowledge is not mere obtaining. Then he considers the second possibility: knowledge is something else different from the form, but notes there is no evidence for that, though some have held it. Finally, he discusses the relational attribute, affirming that consciousness requires a specific relation, but whether that relation needs an additional real or relational or negative factor is not essential. The commentary then explains each point: the first comment distinguishes between identity and essence; the second notes the admission; the third references an earlier refutation; the fourth discusses opposition types; the fifth deals with a potential objection about positivity; the sixth offers a response to the inanimate object objection using shadowy existence; the seventh discusses the lack of evidence; the eighth analyzes the relation between consciousness and relation.

Objection on Self-Knowledge and Its Answers
The author presents an objection against the form theory of knowledge and offers three answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection against the view that knowledge is a form, and the three answers given to resolve it, with particular focus on the answer of knowledge by presence.

Core Idea
Self-knowledge does not require two identical forms because mental existence differs from external existence.

This passage tackles a critical problem for those who define knowledge as a form representing the known. If knowledge is a form, then knowing oneself would seem to require a form of the self identical to the self, leading to two similar things. The author presents three solutions, the most profound being knowledge by presence. Let's examine the objection and each answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and understand the problem
Read the Arabic passage carefully. The objection states that if knowledge is a form, self-knowledge entails a form of the self inhering in the self, causing two similar forms.
The key term 'اجتماع المثلين' means conjunction of two similar things.
2
Identify the first two answers
The first answer distinguishes external from mental existence; the second distinguishes the self as self-subsistent from the form inhering in it.
These answers rely on the categories of existence and inherence.
3
Examine the third answer: knowledge by presence
The third answer claims that knowledge of self is by presence (huduri) not conceptual (husuli), meaning no form is required.
This is the most relied-upon answer according to the commentary.
4
Consider the commentary's critique
The commentary shows weaknesses in the first two answers and confirms the third as the true answer.
The first answer fails if the problem is about the individual form in the soul; the second does not address attributes.
5
Reflect on the significance
Understand why knowledge by presence is a key concept in Islamic epistemology and how it avoids the problem of duplication.
Knowledge by presence is often used for self-knowledge and divine knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن القائل بأن العلم هو الصورة المساوية للمعلوم يرد عليه الإشكال في علم الشيء بذاته وبصفات ذاته، إذ يلزم أن يحل في ذاته صورة مساوية لذاته ولصفاته، وذلك اجتماع المثلين وأجيب عنه تارة بأن ذاته وصفاته موجودات عينية وصورها موجودات ذهنية والمستحيل هو اجتماع عينين متماثلين وأيضاً ذاته قائمة بنفسها، وصورة ذاته قائمة بها، والمستحيل حلول المثلين في محل واحد لا حلول أحدهما في الآخر، وأخرى بأنَّ علم الشيء بذاته وصفاته علم حضوري لا حصولي، ومعنى ذلك أن الحاصلة فإنّه المدعى وفيه أن القائمين بالصورة لا يقولون : إنه حصول الصورة مطلقاً، بل حصول الصورة المجردة للذات المجردة . قوله : ( وصورها موجودات إلخ لو أورد الإشكال بالصورة الشخصية الموجودة في النفس بالوجود الأصيلي لا يكون الجواب موجها . قوله : ( وأيضاً ذاته إلخ هذا الجواب لا يدفع الإشكال بصفات ذاته . قوله : ( وأخرى أي وأجيب تارة أخرى وهذا هو الجواب التحقيقي وعليه التعويل. قوله : ( لا حلول أحدهما في الآخر) الظاهر أن دليل الامتناع عام فالتخصيص تكلف . قوله : ( حضوري لا حصولي فيه بحث وهو أنه إن أراد علم الشيء بذاته وصفاته حضوري
And know that the one who says that knowledge is the form equal to the known is faced with the problem in the knowledge of a thing of its own essence and its attributes, because it would necessarily entail that a form equal to its essence and attributes would inhere in its essence, which would be a conjunction of two similar things. And it is answered sometimes that its essence and attributes are external existents while their forms are mental existents, and the impossible is the conjunction of two identical external things. Also, its essence is self-subsistent, while the form of its essence subsists in it, and the impossible is the inherence of two similar things in one locus, not the inherence of one in the other. And another answer is that the knowledge of a thing of its own essence and attributes is knowledge by presence, not conceptual knowledge, and the meaning of that is that what obtains is indeed what is claimed. But the upholders of the form do not say that it is the obtaining of the form absolutely, but rather the obtaining of the abstract form for the abstract self. His statement: (and their forms are existents etc.) if the problem is posed regarding the individual form existing in the soul by primary existence, the answer is not appropriate. His statement: (also, its essence etc.) this answer does not remove the problem regarding its attributes. His statement: (and another) i.e., it is answered another time, and this is the true answer and on it reliance. His statement: (not the inherence of one in the other) apparently the proof of impossibility is general, so specification is artificial. His statement: (by presence, not conceptual) there is discussion in it: if he means knowledge of its own essence and attributes is by presence [then...]
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage addresses a key objection against the view that knowledge is a form equal to the known: if so, then a thing's knowledge of itself would require a form of its own essence and attributes inhering in itself, leading to two similar forms (i.e., the essence and its form) in one locus. The author presents three answers. First, the essence and attributes are external existents while the forms are mental, and the impossibility is only of two identical external existents. Second, the essence is self-subsistent, and the form subsists in it, so it is not a conjunction in one locus but inherence of one in the other. Third, and most relied upon, is that knowledge of self is by presence (huduri) not by acquisition (husuli). The commentary then critiques each answer: the first answer fails if the problem is about the individual form in the soul by primary existence; the second does not resolve the problem for attributes; the third is the most true. The commentary also notes that the proof of impossibility is general, so specifying that it is not inherence of one in another is artificial. Finally, it raises a discussion about the meaning of knowledge by presence.

ذات مجردة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If knowledge is a form, then one cannot know oneself without having two identical selves.
Correction: The form is mental, not external, and knowledge by presence does not require a form at all.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the most relied-upon answer to the objection about self-knowledge?
A) Distinction between external and mental existence
B) Self is self-subsistent
C) Knowledge by presence
D) The objection is invalid

Key Takeaway
The objection that self-knowledge leads to two identical forms is resolved by the doctrine of knowledge by presence, which avoids the need for a mental form of the self.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own self—your desires, fears, and intentions—do you rely more on mental images and stories, or on a direct, silent awareness? Which one feels closer to the truth, and why does that matter for your accountability to Allah?
?
This lesson argues that knowledge by presence avoids the problem of duplication. In your relationship with Allah, how often do you settle for conceptual knowledge (facts, names, attributes) instead of seeking direct presence through remembrance and reflection? What would change if you prioritised the latter?
?
If self-knowledge is immediate and not a copy, then your identity before Allah is not a mere idea but a living reality you confront. What false 'mental forms' of yourself have you been worshipping—like self-image or reputation—and how can you let direct awareness dismantle them?

What Is Kalam?
beginner
4 min
Objection to Knowledge as Form and Two Initial Answers
Objection against surah theory and two initial answers: existential and locus-based.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection against defining knowledge as a mental form equal to the known, and two early answers that rely on the distinction between extramental and mental existence and the locus of form.

Core Idea
Self-knowledge cannot involve a form identical to the self—that would be impossible.

The definition of knowledge as a form (surah) corresponding to the known seems straightforward, but it runs into trouble when the known is the knowing subject itself. This lesson presents that objection and two initial responses that attempt to rescue the definition by distinguishing between extramental and mental existence, and by clarifying the locus of the form.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the objection and the first two answers. Notice the logical flow: objection, first answer, second answer.
The objection is presented first, then two answers introduced with 'tara' and 'aydan'.
2
Identify the logical problem
Why does self-knowledge lead to social al-mithlayn? The passage says a form equal to the self would need to reside in the self, causing two identical forms.
3
Examine the first answer
The first answer distinguishes between extramental (ayni) and mental (dhihni) existence. The impossibility of two identicals applies only to extramental existents.
The form is mental, the essence is extramental, so no conflict.
4
Examine the second answer
The second answer focuses on the locus: the essence is self-subsistent, the form is subsistent in it. The impossibility is of two forms in one locus, not one form residing in another.
5
Reflect on the effectiveness
Consider whether these answers fully resolve the objection. They show how the surah theory can avoid the immediate logical problem.
These are initial answers; a third answer is given later.

واعلم أن القائل بأن العلم هو الصورة المساوية للمعلوم يرد عليه الإشكال في علم الشيء بذاته وبصفات ذاته، إذ يلزم أن يحل في ذاته صورة مساوية لذاته ولصفاته، وذلك اجتماع المثلين. وأجيب عنه تارة بأن ذاته وصفاته موجودات عينية وصورها موجودات ذهنية والمستحيل هو اجتماع عينين متماثلين. وأيضاً ذاته قائمة بنفسها، وصورة ذاته قائمة بها، والمستحيل حلول المثلين في محل واحد لا حلول أحدهما في الآخر. وأخرى بأن علم الشيء بذاته وصفاته علم حضوري لا حصولي. ومعنى ذلك أن الحاصلة [؟] فإنّه المدعى وفيه أن القائمين بالصورة لا يقولون : إنه حصول الصورة مطلقاً، بل حصول الصورة المجردة للذات المجردة.
Know that the one who says that knowledge is the form equal to the known faces a problem regarding a thing's knowledge of itself and of its attributes, because it would require that a form equal to itself and its attributes resides in itself, and that is the coincidence of two similars. It was answered sometimes: that its essence and attributes are extramental existents, while their forms are mental existents, and the impossible is the coincidence of two identical extramental things. Also, its essence is self-subsistent, and the form of its essence is subsistent in it, and the impossible is the resolution of two similars in one locus, not the resolution of one in the other. And another time: that a thing's knowledge of itself and its attributes is presential knowledge (ilm huduri), not conceptual (husuli). The meaning of that is that the ... [؟] is the claim, and therein that the proponents of the form do not say: it is the obtaining of the form absolutely, but rather the obtaining of the abstract form for the abstract essence.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage presents a classic objection against the definition of knowledge as a mental form (surah) corresponding to the known object, specifically concerning self-knowledge and knowledge of one's own attributes. The objection is that if knowledge is a form, then a thing knowing itself would require that a form identical to itself and its attributes resides in itself, leading to the impossibility of two identical forms (social al-mithlayn). The author then provides two initial answers. The first answer distinguishes between extramental existence (wujud ayni) and mental existence (wujud dhihni): the essence and attributes are extramental, while their forms are mental, so the impossibility of two identical extramental existents does not apply. The second answer focuses on the locus: the essence is self-subsistent, whereas the form of the essence is subsistent in it, and the impossibility is of two similars in a single locus, not one resolving in another. Then a third answer is mentioned: that self-knowledge and knowledge of attributes is presential (huduri), not conceptual (husuli). The passage concludes with a brief critical comment that even the proponents of the form theory do not claim absolute form-obtaining, but rather the obtaining of the abstract form for the abstract essence.

Key Terms
صورة
اجتماع المثلين
موجودات عينية
موجودات ذهنية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the objection is unanswerable and destroys the surah theory.
Correction: The passage shows two early answers that attempt to defend the theory by clarifying metaphysical distinctions.

Checkpoint
What is the main problem with saying knowledge is a form equal to the known when it comes to self-knowledge?
A) It leads to an infinite regress of forms.
B) It would require two identical forms in the same entity, which is impossible.
C) It contradicts the unity of the self.
D) It cannot explain how we know attributes.

Key Takeaway
The objection of social al-mithlayn challenges the surah theory of knowledge, and the first two answers use the distinction between extramental and mental existence and the difference between self-subsistence and form-subsistence to avoid the impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own shortcomings, do you tend to treat your mental image of yourself as the real you? How does the distinction between mental and extramental existence help you see your self-knowledge more honestly—and what would it mean to stop confusing your self-image with your actual self?
?
The passage says that if knowledge were a perfect copy, self-knowledge would be impossible. Yet you know yourself, however imperfectly. Are you willing to admit that your knowledge of yourself is not a perfect copy but a relationship? How does that affect how you try to improve yourself?
?
If you cannot even have an exact mental copy of yourself, how can you ever claim to have a perfect concept of Allah in your mind? Does this lesson shake your reliance on your own ideas about Allah—and force you to seek knowledge of Him through His revelation instead of mental pictures?
