The Presential Knowledge Answer and Its Implication
Third answer: self-knowledge is presential; concluding remark about form-theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third answer to the objection: that self-knowledge is presential (huduri) not conceptual (husuli), and the critical remark that even form-theorists do not claim absolute form-obtaining.

Core Idea
Self-knowledge is presential, not representational, so no form is needed.

The first two answers attempted to save the surah theory by reinterpreting the locus of the form. But a third, more radical answer is offered: self-knowledge is not conceptual knowledge at all—it is presential knowledge (ilm huduri), which does not involve a mental form. The passage concludes with a critical observation that even proponents of the surah theory do not claim that any form is obtained absolutely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the third answer and the concluding remark. Note that this is presented as 'ukhra' (another time).
The phrase 'الحاصلة [؟]' is uncertain due to OCR.
2
Understand presential vs conceptual knowledge
Presential knowledge (ilm huduri) involves direct presence of the known, eliminating the need for a separate form. Conceptual knowledge (ilm husuli) relies on a mental form.
3
Note the critical remark
The passage states that even form-theorists do not say knowledge is the obtaining of a form absolutely, but rather the obtaining of an abstract form for an abstract essence.
This suggests the objection may arise from an unwarranted generalization.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider whether this third answer fully resolves the objection or whether it changes the very definition of knowledge being defended.

Knowledge as Presence and the Solution to Self-Knowledge
The author defines presential knowledge and resolves the self-knowledge paradox.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the concept of presential knowledge (al-ʿilm al-ḥuḍūrī) resolves the paradox of self-knowledge through the notion of distinction by consideration (al-taghāyur bi-l-iʿtibār).

Core Idea
Self-knowledge is possible because one essence can have two aspects.

How can something know itself if knowledge requires a relation between two distinct things? This lesson presents the answer from Islamic epistemology: presential knowledge. The author explains that the soul has two capacities—to know and to be known—which provide the needed distinction without splitting the essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on presential knowledge
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the author's claim that in self-knowledge, the knower, known, and knowledge are unified.
Focus on the phrase 'يتحد العاقل والمعقول والعقل'.
2
Identify the objection
The author raises an objection: presence requires two distinct things. How then can a thing be present to itself? Locate this objection in the text.
It begins with 'فإن قلت'.
3
Understand the answer: distinction by consideration
The author answers that a distinction by consideration (التغاير بالاعتبار) suffices. The soul's capacity to be knower and capacity to be known are distinct in meaning, even though the soul is one entity.
This is the key solution.
4
Reflect on the application
Consider how this applies to the soul and to God. The passage notes that the same logic holds for the Necessary Being.
Notice the phrase 'وكذا الحال في الواجب تعالى'.
5
Confirm your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to test your grasp of the argument.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المعلوم هاهنا حاضر عند العالم بنفسه لا بحصول صورته، ففي علم الشيء بذاته يتحد العاقل والمعقول والعقل في الوجود العيني، وفي علمه بصفاته يتحد العقل والمعقول فيه، فإن قلت: كيف يتصور حضور الشيء عند نفسه مع أنَّ الحضور نسبة لا تتصور إلا بين شيئين؟ قلت : إنَّ التغاير بالاعتبار كاف لتحقق النسبة، ولا شك أنَّ النفس من حيث أنها صالحة لأن تكون عالمة بشيء من الأشياء، مغايرة لها من حيث أنها صالحة لأن تكون معلومة لشيء ما، وبهذا التغاير أيضاً يندفع الإشكال في علم الشيء بنفسه
قوله : ( ولا شك أن النفس) يعني أن النفس الناطقة لها جهتان صلاحية العالمية المطلقة وصلاحية المعلومية المطلقة وهما متغايرتان إذ توجد الثانية في الصور العقلية دون الأولى وتحقق الجهتين كاف لحصول النسبة ولا يقتضي التغاير بالذات وتحقيقه أن النفس الناطقة أنها ماهية مجردة علم ومن حيث أن ماهيتها مجردة قائمة بذاتها عالم ومن حيث أن لها ماهية مجردة معلومة وكذا الحال في الواجب تعالى
وقال الشيخ في المباحثات : لكل شخص حقيقة وشخصية وتلك الشخصية زائدة على الماهية على ما مضى ثم إن كانت الحقيقة مقتضية لتلك الشخصية كان ذلك النوع منحصراً في ذلك الشخص وإلا وقعت الكثرة فيه، ولا شك أن تلك الحقيقة مغايرة للمجموع الحاصل من تلك الحقيقة، وتلك الشخصية ولما تحقق هذا القدر من المغايرة كفى ذلك في حصول الإضافة انتهى، وفيه أنه لا يجري في علمه تعالى بذاته لكون ذاتها أيضاً ذاته وكذا أنه لا يجري في علم النفس بخصوص تشخصه
قوله : ( يندفع) الإشكال أي كما اندفع عن القائل بأن العلم حضور الماهية المجردة عند عين الذات المجردة إما بنفسها أو بمثالها البتة، فالظاهر أنه مكابرة ضرورة جواز تصورنا صفاتنا على نحو تصورنا صفات غيرنا وإن أراد أنه قد يكون حضورياً، فذلك على تقدير تسليمه لا يدفع الاعتراض المورد في صورة العلم الحصولي للشيء بصفاته فتأمل . قوله : ففي علم الشيء بذاته يتحد وذلك لأن العلم هو الأمر الحاصل للعالم، والحاصل في العلم الحضوري نفس المعلوم لا صورته كما في العلم الحصولي فالشجاعة القائمة بالنفس من حيث قيامها بها ،علم ومن حيث هي هي معلوم، وبهذا ظهر أن منشأ السؤال بقوله : فإن قلت : كيف يتصور إلخ؟ ليس كون العلم الحضوري عبارة عن نفس الحضور كما توهم وإلا لا يتصور اتحاده لا مع العالم ولا مع المعلوم بل يتضمنه هذا الحضور فتدبر. :قوله من حيث أنها صالحة) جعل التغاير الموقوف عليه لتحقق نسبة الحضور تحقق السبق ضرورة واتفاقاً بصلوح العالمية والمعلومية لا نفسها لأن كلاً منهما متأخر عن ذلك التغاير بمرتبتين. واعلم أن المراد بالتغاير الاعتباري في أمثاله هو المقابل للتغاير الذاتي، لا الناشيء من محض اعتبار المعتبر، حتى يرد أن الكلام في أحوال الأشياء في أنفسها لا أحوالها، بحسب اعتبار المعتبر ألا يرى أنَّ صلاحية العالمية وصلاحية المعلومية ثابتة له في نفس الأمر.
The known here is present to the knower by its essence, not by acquisition of its image. So in a thing's knowledge of its essence, the knower, the known, and the knowledge are unified in external existence. In its knowledge of its attributes, knowledge and the known are unified. If you say: How can a thing be present to itself, given that presence is a relation only conceivable between two things? I say: Distinction by consideration (al-taghāyur bi-l-iʿtibār) is sufficient for the relation to hold. There is no doubt that the soul, inasmuch as it is capable of being knowing of something, is distinct from itself inasmuch as it is capable of being known by something. And by this distinction the difficulty concerning a thing's knowledge of itself is also resolved.
His statement: (And there is no doubt that the soul) means that the rational soul has two aspects: the capacity for absolute knowing and the capacity for absolute being known. They are distinct, because the second occurs in mental forms while the first does not. The actuality of the two aspects suffices for the relation to obtain, and does not require distinction by essence. Its verification: the rational soul is a pure quiddity; inasmuch as its quiddity is pure and self-subsistent, it is knower; and inasmuch as it has a pure quiddity, it is known. The same holds for the Necessary (al-Wājib), exalted.
And the Shaykh said in al-Mubāḥathāt: Every person has an essence (ḥaqīqa) and an individuality (shakhṣiyya), and that individuality is additional to the quiddity, as previously mentioned. Then if the essence necessitates that individuality, the species is confined to that person; otherwise multiplicity occurs. There is no doubt that the essence is distinct from the composite consisting of that essence and that individuality. Since this degree of distinction is realized, it suffices for the relation to hold. End. Its rebuttal: It does not apply to His, exalted, knowledge of His essence, because His essence is also His essence; likewise it does not apply to the soul's knowledge of its particular individuation.
His statement: (is resolved) the difficulty—i.e., just as it is resolved for the one who says that knowledge is the presence of the pure quiddity at the very essence of the pure essence, either by itself or by its image, absolutely. So the apparent is that it is a false claim, because it is necessary that we can conceive our attributes in the same way we conceive the attributes of others. If he intends that it may be presential, then that, assuming its acceptance, does not repel the objection raised regarding the case of acquired knowledge (al-ʿilm al-ḥuṣūlī) of a thing's attributes. So reflect. His statement: For in a thing's knowledge of its essence, unification occurs—that is because knowledge is what is attained by the knower, and what is attained in presential knowledge is the known itself, not its image as in acquired knowledge. So courage, subsisting in the soul, inasmuch as it subsists in it, is knowledge; and inasmuch as it is what it is, it is known. From this it becomes clear that the source of the question, 'How can it be imagined?' etc., is not that presential knowledge is merely presence, as is imagined; otherwise its unification with neither the knower nor the known would be conceivable. Rather, this presence implies it. So ponder. His statement: (inasmuch as it is capable) makes the distinction on which the reality of the relation of presence depends necessary and agreed upon by the capability of being knowing and being known, not by the capabilities themselves, because each of them is two levels posterior to that distinction. And know that what is meant by 'distinction by consideration' (al-taghāyur al-iʿtibārī) in such cases is the opposite of essential distinction, not arising from mere consideration of the considerer—otherwise it would be said that the discussion concerns the states of things in themselves, not their states according to the considerer's consideration. Do you not see that the capability of being knowing and the capability of being known are established for it in actual fact?
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation and defense of the doctrine that knowledge is the presence of the known to the knower. It begins by stating that in presential knowledge (al-ʿilm al-ḥuḍūrī), the known is present by its essence, not by a mental image. Consequently, when a thing knows itself, the knower, the known, and knowledge are unified in external existence; but when it knows its attributes, only knowledge and the known are unified (since the attribute is not the essence). The author then raises an objection: how can something be present to itself when presence implies a relation between two distinct things? The answer is that a distinction by consideration (al-taghāyur bi-l-iʿtibār) suffices: the soul has two aspects—its capacity to be a knower and its capacity to be known—and these are distinct even though the soul is one entity. The commentator elaborates that the rational soul is a pure quiddity; from one aspect it is a knower, from another it is known, and this holds for God as well. The passage then brings in a quote from 'the Shaykh in al-Mubāḥathāt' about the distinction between essence and individuality, arguing that this distinction also provides a basis for the relation in self-knowledge, but notes that it does not apply to God's knowledge of His essence (since His essence is identical to His attributes) nor to the soul's knowledge of its unique individuation. Further commentary clarifies that the difficulty is resolved for those who hold knowledge to be presence, but not entirely for acquired knowledge of attributes. The author emphasizes that in presential knowledge, the known itself is present, not an image, and that the questioner's error is to think that presential knowledge is mere presence rather than including the known. Finally, the notion of 'distinction by consideration' is defined as a real distinction in the thing itself, not a mere mental construct, so the discussion remains about objective states.

Key Terms
العلم الحضوري
التغاير بالاعتبار
النفس الناطقة
صلاحية العالمية
صلاحية المعلومية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Self-knowledge is impossible because it would require the self to be two different things.
Correction: Self-knowledge is possible because the self has two aspects (knowing and known) that are distinct in consideration, not in essence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how can a thing know itself if knowledge requires a relation between two things?
A) By having a mental image of itself
B) By a distinction of aspects in the same essence, such as capacity to know versus capacity to be known
C) By acquiring an image from an external source
D) It is impossible; the premise is false

Key Takeaway
Self-knowledge is explained by the soul having two distinct capacities—to know and to be known—which provide the required relation without dividing the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your own self-awareness, do you feel a tension between being the one who knows and the one who is known? How does this passage challenge you to see that tension as a sign of one reality, not two?
?
The author says a distinction in consideration is enough. Is there a conflict in your life where you have been waiting for a real, external separation to resolve it, when the true solution might be a shift in how you consider yourself? What would it cost you to make that shift today?
?
If your soul is both knowing and known, which capacity do you neglect more—actively knowing yourself through honest self-reflection, or allowing yourself to be known by others and by God? Which one are you avoiding, and what would it mean for your iman to embrace it this week?

Knowledge as Relation or Attribute: The Problem of Non-Existent Objects
The author discusses two views of knowledge and resolves the problem of non-existents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two views on the nature of knowledge (pure relation or real attribute) and how they address the problem of knowing non-existent external objects.

Core Idea
Knowledge of non-existents is possible via mental existence or mere distinction.

This passage introduces a central debate in Islamic epistemology: Is knowledge a pure relation (iḍāfa) or a real attribute? The problem arises for knowledge of non-existent objects—if knowledge requires a real term, how can we know something that does not exist? The author presents two solutions: mental existence or a distinction that does not require existence. Let's read the source.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two views
Read the first part of the passage and note the two positions: knowledge as pure relation or as real attribute entailing relation.
These are the starting points.
2
Understand the objection
What is the objection concerning knowledge of external non-existents? The relation needs a real term, but non-existents are not real.
3
Analyze the first solution
The first solution appeals to mental existence: non-existents exist in the mind, so the relation is to a mental entity. This is al-Rāzī's view.
The story of the inanimate object is used against mental image theory.
4
Analyze the second solution
The second solution holds that relation depends only on distinction (imtiyāz), not on actual existence of the two things. Distinction can be between two non-existents.
This avoids the need for any existence.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider how these solutions preserve the possibility of knowing non-existents, and how this relates to God's knowledge.

Concerning the one who says that knowledge is a pure relation (iḍāfa maḥḍa) or a real attribute (ṣifa ḥaqīqiyya) entailing relation. As for the objection raised against him regarding knowledge of external non-existents, it is repelled from him either by consideration of mental existence (al-wujūd al-dhihnī), as Imām al-Rāzī held in al-Mabāḥith al-Mashriqiyya, claiming that knowledge is a specific relation, not a mental image, because of the well-known story of the inanimate object; or by [the claim] that the relation depends on distinction (imtiyāz), which does not depend on the existence of the two distinct things either in the external world or in the mind.
His statement: (And as for the objection against him) i.e., against the aforementioned claimant. As for the one who says that it is the present image, there is no objection against him because non-existents exist in the mind.
His statement: (the story of the inanimate object) which is that the quiddity of blackness is present in it without knowledge. So if knowledge were the image present in the thing, the inanimate object would be knowing blackness.
His statement: (absolutely) i.e., whether both are theoretical or intuitive, and whether disconnection between them is permissible or not.
His statement: (And by this distinction the difficulty is also resolved) Its apparent meaning indicates the resolution of the difficulty concerning a thing's knowledge of itself for the one who says that it is a real attribute entailing relation, and it is only by the realization of the distinction by consideration. And he will clearly state in the section on divine matters (al-Ilāhiyyāt) in proving the universality of God's knowledge that knowledge, assuming it is a real attribute having a relation, does not necessitate any distinction between the knower and the known at all, because the relation that requires the two relata is only between that attribute and [one of them], not between the knower and the known, so as to necessitate their distinction, even by consideration. Yes, if there were a relation between that attribute and [something else], then a relation between the knower and the known would require distinction between them, even if by [that relation]?
His statement: (it is repelled from him either by consideration of mental existence) if the source of the objection against him is the precondition of the relation by the existence of the related term, then its repulsion by consideration of mental existence is a subject of research, because the apparent...
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage deals with the objection raised against those who define knowledge as a pure relation or as a real attribute that entails a relation. The specific problem is knowledge of non-existent external objects: if knowledge is a relation, it requires a real term, but non-existents are not real. Two solutions are offered. The first, attributed to Imām al-Rāzī, invokes mental existence: the non-existent object exists in the mind, so the relation is to a mental entity. The second solution holds that the relation depends only on a distinction (imtiyāz) between the knower and the known, and that distinction does not require the external or mental existence of the two terms—only that they be distinct. The commentary then expands: for those who hold that knowledge is a present image (al-ṣūra al-ḥāḍira), non-existents pose no problem because they are mentally existent. The 'story of the inanimate object' is cited to argue against knowledge being a mental image: if it were, an inanimate object would 'know' blackness because it has the quiddity of blackness. Further, the commentator notes that a later passage in the section on divine matters (al-Ilāhiyyāt) will clarify that if knowledge is a real attribute with a relation, the relation is between that attribute and the known, not between the knower and the known, so no distinction is required between knower and known. Finally, the commentator casts doubt on the mental-existence solution, implying it is not fully satisfactory.

الصفة الحقيقية
الوجود الذهني
الامتياز
العلم بالمعدومات

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that knowledge of non-existents is impossible because relation requires a real term.
Correction: The passage shows that knowledge of non-existents is possible either because they have mental existence, or because relation only requires distinction, not existence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the second solution to the objection regarding knowledge of external non-existents?
A) They are known through their effects
B) The relation depends on distinction which does not require existence
C) They are known through divine revelation
D) They are not truly known

Key Takeaway
The problem of knowing non-existent objects is resolved either by positing mental existence or by arguing that relation requires only distinction, not existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you worried about a future event that hasn't happened yet. Did you mentally picture it as if it were real? How does this lesson's distinction between mental existence and pure relation change how you view that worry? Write down a commitment to treat that future non-existent as a mere distinction rather than a loaded mental picture.
?
If God's knowledge of you is a pure relation that does not depend on your existence, how does that affect your sense of being truly known by Him? Are you comfortable with the idea that His knowledge is a relation to your distinctness, not a mental image? Pinpoint one fear you have about being unknown and ask yourself if this passage alleviates it.
?
You have likely acted based on knowledge of something that does not exist—a memory of a past sin, or a concept like 'justice'. Did you ever consider that this knowledge might be just a relation to a mental existence or a distinction? Reflect: is your attachment to that knowledge more about mental images or about a real relation? What would change if you shifted to the latter view?

What Is Kalam?
beginner

Can One Created Knowledge Relate to Two Knowns? The First Two Schools
The author presents the first two of four schools on whether a single temporal knowledge can relate to two knowns in detail.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first two positions on whether a single temporal knowledge can relate to two distinct knowns in detail, and the refutation of the first position.

Core Idea
Single created knowledge cannot relate to two knowns in detail, unlike God's knowledge.

This passage plunges into a classic kalam debate: can one instance of created knowledge encompass two distinct pieces of information? The question is restricted to temporal knowledge because God's eternal knowledge is known to relate to infinite things. The author outlines four schools; here we examine the first two: the permissive view of some Ash'arites and the absolute negation of Ash'ari himself and many Mu'tazila.

Guided Path
1
Understand the question
Note that the debate concerns temporal knowledge (not eternal) and 'detailed' knowledge, not summary knowledge. Recognise that summary knowledge can relate to multiplicity without issue.
2
First school: analogy from God's knowledge
Read the first school's argument: some Ash'arites permit it by analogy to God's knowledge, which is one yet relates to many knowns.
The passage labels this position as 'first' and attributes it to 'some of our companions from the Ash'arites'.
3
The refutation: invalid analogy and power problem
The author immediately rejects the analogy as a false comparison of the unseen to the witnessed without a common factor. Then a second objection: if we applied the same reasoning to power, we would contradict Ash'arite doctrine, since temporal power cannot relate to two objects of power.
Note the argument that there is no relevant difference between knowledge and power in this regard.
4
Second school: absolute negation
The second school, attributed to Abū al-Ḥasan al-Ashʿarī and many Muʿtazila, holds that it is absolutely not permissible. The passage breaks off, likely giving their argument.
This position is the opposite extreme: no single created knowledge can relate to two knowns in detail.
5
Reflect on the disagreement
Consider why these two schools differ: the first relies on an analogy from the divine, while the second denies it altogether. Think about the implications for how knowledge is understood.
The remaining two schools will be covered in later lessons.

[The Second Goal: One Knowledge and Its Relation to Two Knowns]
(A single knowledge—qualified by temporality, because a single eternal knowledge may relate to infinite things—may it relate to two knowns?) i.e., in a detailed manner, for there is no disagreement that a single summary knowledge (al-ʿilm al-ijmālī) relates to what contains multiplicity. (Concerning it there are four schools.) (The first is of some of our companions—from the Ashʿarites—they permit that absolutely, like the knowledge of God, exalted, for it is one knowledge related to multiple items of knowledge. (We say: this is an analogy and comparison of the witnessed to the absent, without a common factor; so it is invalid. Also, it necessitates for those who argue from our companions by that regarding [divine] power, for temporal power does not relate to two objects of power according to our principle, as will come that eternal power cannot relate to two or more objects of power, and the distinction between knowledge and power in that is impossible. (The second is the doctrine of Shaykh Abū al-Ḥasan al-Ashʿarī and many of the Muʿtazila: that it is absolutely not permissible, for...)
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage introduces a new topic: whether a single temporal knowledge can relate to two distinct knowns in detail. The author notes that this is restricted to temporal knowledge because a single eternal knowledge (like God's) can relate to infinite things. He then states that there are four schools on this issue. This lesson covers the first two. The first school, held by some Ashʿarites, permits it absolutely, analogizing from God's knowledge: just as one divine knowledge relates to many things, so can temporal knowledge. The objection ('We say') refutes this: the analogy from the unseen (God) to the witnessed (created) is invalid without a common factor. Moreover, if this reasoning were applied to power, it would lead to a contradiction, because temporal power cannot relate to two objects of power in Ashʿarite doctrine, and there is no relevant distinction between knowledge and power in this regard. The second school, attributed to Abū al-Ḥasan al-Ashʿarī himself and many Muʿtazila, holds that it is absolutely not permissible. The text breaks off at 'for...', likely giving their argument.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Because God's knowledge is one and relates to many, created knowledge must also be able to relate to multiple knowns in detail.
Correction: The analogy is invalid because there is no common factor between God's knowledge and created knowledge, and it would lead to a contradiction regarding power.

Checkpoint
What is the first school's argument for a single knowledge relating to two knowns?
A) It is possible because God's knowledge is one but relates to many
B) It is impossible because knowledge is an attribute
C) It is possible only for theoretical knowledge
D) It is impossible because it would require division

Key Takeaway
The first school's analogy from God's knowledge is invalid, and the second school holds that a single created knowledge cannot relate to two knowns in detail.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because something is true of God, it must be true of His creation? The author rejected the analogy from God's knowledge. Reflect on a time you made such an assumption. How could you be more careful to avoid comparing God's attributes to human attributes without a valid basis?
?
Al-Ashʿarī himself differed from some of his followers. This shows that even revered scholars can be corrected or have nuanced views. Are you willing to examine your own beliefs critically, even if they come from a trusted authority? What would it take for you to change a deeply held belief if the evidence contradicted it?
?
The analogy used by the first school lacked a common factor. In your daily life, do you sometimes use comparisons to justify your positions without carefully checking if the comparison is valid? How can you cultivate intellectual honesty and humility in your arguments?

What Is Kalam?
intermediate

Definition of Compound Ignorance and the Mu'tazilite Objection
Al-Jurjani defines compound ignorance, proves it opposite to knowledge, and refutes Mu'tazilite similarity claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of compound ignorance, why al-Jurjani considers it opposite to knowledge, and the Mu'tazilite argument that it is similar to knowledge, along with the response.

Core Idea
Compound ignorance is opposite to knowledge, not similar, despite the Mu'tazilite claim.

Al-Jurjani begins by defining compound ignorance, a key concept in kalam. He argues that it is opposite to knowledge because they cannot coexist and are maximally different. The Mu'tazilites object, claiming similarity based on two proofs. This lesson unpacks the definition, the proof of opposition, and the Mu'tazilite objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Understand what compound ignorance is: a firm, stable belief that does not correspond to reality, whether from doubt or blind following. Note why it is called 'compound'.
Stability is not a condition in compound ignorance according to al-Jurjani.
2
Grasp the proof of opposition
Al-Jurjani states that compound ignorance is opposite to knowledge because they are two existent meanings that cannot coexist in the same subject and have extreme difference.
Opposites in kalam require both to be existent, not mere negations.
3
Examine the first Mu'tazilite proof
The Mu'tazilites argue that the only distinction is in the relation of correspondence to the object, which is external, so they share the same essence (firm belief).
They claim similarity, not opposition.
4
Examine the second Mu'tazilite proof
A single continuous belief can be knowledge at one time and compound ignorance later (e.g., belief about Zayd's location). This transformation would be impossible if they were essentially different.
This relies on the belief being essentially unchanged.
5
Review the responses
Notice al-Jurjani's commentary refutes these proofs: correspondence is not external, and continuity may involve renewal of similar instances.
The responses show that the Mu'tazilite arguments fail to prove essential sameness.

[Third Objective: Compound Ignorance]. Compound ignorance is an expression of a firm, stable belief that does not correspond to reality, whether it is based on a doubt or blind following. So stability is not considered in compound ignorance as is well-known in the books. It is called 'compound' because he believes a thing contrary to what it is, so that is ignorance of that thing, and he believes that he believes it as it is, so that is another ignorance; they are combined together. (And it is opposite to knowledge because the definition of opposites applies to them: they are two meanings, existent, impossible to coexist in one subject, and there is extreme difference between them as well. (And the Mu'tazilites said:) i.e., many of them, (that compound ignorance is not opposite to knowledge, but it is similar to it. So the impossibility of their coexistence is due to similarity, not opposition. They claimed similarity between them for two reasons: First: The distinction between them is only with respect to the object, i.e., that distinguishing relation between them is correspondence or non-correspondence. For knowledge corresponds to its object, and compound ignorance does not correspond to it. (And the relation does not enter into the essence of the two relata) because the relation is posterior to its two terms, so it is external to them. (And distinction by external matters does not necessitate difference in essence). Since there is no difference between them except in this aspect, they must share the complete essence. Second: One who believes from morning to evening that Zayd is in the house, and Zayd was in it until noon, then left, has a single continuous belief from morning to evening. That belief does not differ in essence and reality necessarily. Then that belief was first knowledge, then turned into compound ignorance. (And transformation) from one thing to another cannot be conceived except in a thing that is one in essence. So if knowledge and compound ignorance were different in essence, it would not be permissible for one to turn into the other. His saying: 'And the relation does not enter...' contains that the necessary consequence is the exclusion of correspondence and non-correspondence from the believer and his belief, not their exclusion from the essence of knowledge and compound ignorance. His saying: 'in the complete essence' i.e., firm belief. His saying: 'continuous' even if by renewal of similar instances. The belief. His saying: 'so this is another ignorance that has combined' – he did not intend by what he mentioned that the two simple ignorances themselves became parts of compound ignorance, for then it would not be correct to say that the whole is opposite to a thing while each of its parts is non-existent. Rather, he intended that in the form of compound ignorance, two simple ignorances exist, even if they are not parts of it – understand. His saying: 'they are two existent meanings' indicates that existence considered in the definition of opposites in their view means that negation is not part of their concept, not that they exist externally, for knowledge has no existence according to what the author indicated here – his chosen opinion that it is expression of relation and addition. His saying: 'the relation is posterior to its two terms' – we have previously objected to this well-known premise in the discussion of necessity, that a set of relations has a relation to each one of them, and that relation is not posterior to each of its terms necessarily because it is included in the set of relations. The answer to it also – let it be considered.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is a definition, proof, and objection-and-answer passage concerning compound ignorance (al-jahl al-murakkab). It begins by defining compound ignorance as a firm, stable belief that does not correspond to reality, whether based on doubt or blind following. The definition notes that stability is not considered a necessary condition in compound ignorance, contrary to what is well-known. It is called 'compound' because two ignorances combine: ignorance of the thing as it is, and the belief that one knows it correctly. The author then states that compound ignorance is opposite (ḍidd) to knowledge, because they satisfy the definition of opposites: both are existent meanings (not mere negations), they cannot coexist in the same subject, and there is extreme difference between them. The Mu'tazilites (many of them) object, claiming that compound ignorance is not opposite but similar (mumāthil) to knowledge. Their argument is that the impossibility of coexistence is due to similarity, not opposition. They give two proofs. The first proof: the only distinction between knowledge and compound ignorance is the relation of correspondence or non-correspondence to the object, but relation is external to the essence of the two relata, and distinguishing by external matters does not necessitate difference in essence. Therefore, they must share the complete essence (firm belief). The second proof: a continuous firm belief, unchanged in essence, can be knowledge at one time and compound ignorance at another (e.g., believing Zayd is in the house from morning to evening, when Zayd leaves at noon). Transformation of one into the other would be impossible if they were essentially different. The passage then presents commentary responses (qawluhu...) that engage these proofs. The first response clarifies that the Mu'tazilite argument forces exclusion of correspondence/non-correspondence from the believer and belief, not from the essence of knowledge and ignorance. The second notes that 'continuous' may involve renewal of similar instances. The third explains that the two simple ignorances are not parts of compound ignorance but coexist in it. The fourth clarifies that 'existent meanings' in the definition of opposites means negation is not part of their concept, not that they exist externally (since knowledge is a relation for the author). Finally, there is a critique of the Mu'tazilite premise that relation is posterior to its terms, referencing a previous argument on necessity.

Key Terms
الجهل المركب
الضد
المماثلة
المطابقة
الاعتقاد الجازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Compound ignorance is similar to knowledge because both are firm beliefs, differing only in external relation.
Correction: Al-Jurjani argues that correspondence is not external to the essence, and the transformation argument fails because the belief is not identical over time.

Checkpoint
Why do the Mu'tazilites claim that compound ignorance is similar to knowledge rather than opposite?
A) Because they both require a stable belief
B) Because the only difference is in correspondence to the object, which is external to the essence
C) Because compound ignorance cannot exist in the same subject as knowledge
D) Because knowledge is a relation while ignorance is a negation

Key Takeaway
Compound ignorance is a firm belief that does not correspond to reality, making it opposite to knowledge, not similar as the Mu'tazilites claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have at some point been certain of something that was false—that is compound ignorance. How does this lesson challenge your confidence in the things you currently hold as firm beliefs? Will you now examine them more closely?
?
When you cling to a sin, thinking you are fine, you are suffering from compound ignorance about your own state. What specific belief about yourself do you need to test for correspondence with reality today?
?
The Mu'tazilites argued that a belief that changes must be the same essence. But al-Jurjani says it is renewed. When you repent, do you think your renewed faith is a return to the same old self, or a new creation? What does that imply about the necessity of ongoing renewal?

Definition of Compound Ignorance and Its Opposition to Knowledge
Defines compound ignorance and states it is the opposite of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of compound ignorance and why it is considered an opposite of knowledge in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Compound ignorance is two ignorances in one, and it opposes knowledge as a true opposite.

Compound ignorance is a key concept in Islamic epistemology. It describes a person who not only holds a false belief but is also unaware of his own ignorance. This passage defines it and establishes its status as a genuine opposite of knowledge, using the criteria for opposites common in kalam. Understanding this distinction is foundational for discussions on knowledge and belief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own words and the commentator's clarifications come through before you interpret.
The passage combines a definition with commentary, so expect two layers.
2
Identify the two components of compound ignorance
Notice that compound ignorance involves two simple ignorances: ignorance of the truth and ignorance of one's own ignorance. The text says 'two simple ignorances exist' even if they are not literal parts.
These two ignorances are simultaneous, not sequential.
3
Understand the proof of opposition
The passage argues that compound ignorance is a true opposite (ضد) of knowledge by applying the criteria: both are existential meanings that cannot coexist in the same subject and have utmost difference. Note the nuance that 'existential' here means negation is not part of the concept.
This connects to broader kalam discussions on the nature of knowledge and its absence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثاني - المقصد الثالث : الجهل المركب الشيء، ويعتقد أنه يعتقده على ما هو عليه، فهذا جهل آخر قد تركبا معاً، (وهو ضد للعلم لصدق حد الضدين عليهما ، فإنهما معنيان وموجوديان يستحيل اجتماعهما في محل واحد، وبينهما غاية الخلاف أيضاً) قوله : (فهذا جهل آخر قد تركب معاً : لم يرد بما ذكره أنَّ الجهلين البسيطين بعينهما وقعا جزأين من الجهل المركب وإلا لم يستقم القول بأن الكل ضد الشيء على كون كل من جزئيه عدماً، بل أراد أنَّ في صورة الجهل المركب وجد جهلان بسيطان وإن لم يكونا جزأين منه فافهم . قوله : ( فإنهما معنيان وجوديان هذا يشعر بأنَّ الوجودية المعتبرة في تعريف المتضادين عندهم بمعنى أن لا يكون السلب جزءاً من مفهومهما لا بمعنى أن يكونا موجودين في الخارج، إذ لا وجود للعلم على ما أشار المصنف هاهنا إلى ما اختاره من كونه عبارة عن نفس التعلق والإضافة.
The second type - the third objective: Compound ignorance. [A person] believes the thing and thinks that he believes it as it is, so this is another ignorance that has compounded together. And it is an opposite of knowledge, because the definition of opposites applies to them both: they are two existential meanings impossible to coexist in one subject, and there is utmost opposition between them. He said (i.e., the commentator): 'This is another ignorance that has compounded together' – he did not mean by what he mentioned that the two simple ignorances themselves have become parts of composite ignorance, otherwise the statement that the whole is opposite of the thing would not be valid given that each of its parts is a non-existence. Rather, he meant that in the case of compound ignorance, two simple ignorances exist, even if they are not parts of it; so understand. He said: 'For they are two existential meanings' – this indicates that the existentiality considered in the definition of opposites for them means that negation is not part of their concept, not that they exist externally, for knowledge has no existence according to what the author indicated here, choosing that it is a expression for relation and attribution itself.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage defines compound ignorance and argues that it is an opposite (ضد) of knowledge. It begins by stating that compound ignorance is when a person believes something and thinks that he believes it correctly (i.e., he believes something contrary to reality and is convinced that his belief is true). This yields two ignorances: simple ignorance of the truth, and simple ignorance of one's own ignorance—hence 'compound.' The author then asserts that this compound ignorance is a true opposite of knowledge, citing the criteria for opposites: two existent meanings that cannot coexist in the same subject, and which have the greatest difference. The commentary clarifies that the two simple ignorances do not literally become parts of the compound; rather, two separate simple ignorances are present in the person. It also explains that 'existent' in the definition of opposites means 'not conceptually including negation,' not external existence, since knowledge is a relation, not an entity.

الضد
العلم

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think compound ignorance is just simple ignorance repeated, or that the two simple ignorances literally merge into one.
Correction: The passage stresses that two distinct simple ignorances exist simultaneously without merging, and they are not literal parts of a composite whole.

Simple Ignorance: Definition and Related Concepts
Al-Jurjani defines simple ignorance and distinguishes it from related cognitive states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what simple ignorance is, how it differs from compound ignorance, and how it relates to oversight, heedlessness, distraction, and forgetfulness.

Core Idea
Simple ignorance is the absence of knowledge from one capable of it, not a direct contrary.

Al-Jurjani moves from the topic of knowledge to its privative opposite: simple ignorance. Before discussing the states that resemble ignorance, he clarifies that simple ignorance is not a contrary of knowledge but rather an absence of it in a subject that should possess it. This precise distinction is crucial for later discussions of accountability and error.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of simple ignorance
Read the opening sentence carefully. Simple ignorance is defined as 'absence of knowledge in one who is capable of it'. Note that it is not a contrary (ḍidd) but a privative opposite ('adam al-malaka).
This distinction is central: blindness is a privative opposite of sight, not a positive quality.
2
Identify the related terms: sahwu, ghaflah, dhuhul
The passage lists three states that are 'similar to' simple ignorance. For each, note how they are caused by instability of the mental concept or lack of attention.
These are not identical to simple ignorance but share a similar privative character.
3
Analyze the distinction between sahwu and nisyān
The text ends with a precise distinction: oversight (sahwu) is when the image leaves the active perceiver but remains in memory; forgetfulness (nisyān) is when it leaves both.
This shows how subtle differences in cognitive states are categorized in the Islamic tradition.
4
Connect to the Qur'anic example
Read the verse about mothers forgetting their infants on the Day of Judgment. Reflect on how dhuhul (distraction) is exemplified by extreme bewilderment.
The verse supports the idea that dhuhul is a type of sahwu caused by overwhelming confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الرابع : الجهل البسيط ( الجهل يقال للمركب، وهو ما ذكرناه و يقال أيضاً للبسيط، وهو عدم العلم عما من شأنه أن يكون عالماً ، فلا يكون ضداً للعلم، بل مقابلاً له مقابلة العدم للملكة ) ويقرب منه أي من الجهل البسيط السهو وهو كالجهل بسيط سببه عدم استثبات التصور أي العلم تصورياً كان أو تصديقياً فإنه إذا لم يتمكن التصور، ولم يتقرر كان في معرض الزوال فيثبت مرة ويزول أخرى، ويثبت بدله تصور آخر فيشتبه أحدهما بالآخر اشتباهاً غير مستقر حتى إذا نبه الساهي أدنى تنبيه تنبه وعاد إليه التصور الأول وكذلك الغفلة تقرب من الجهل أيضاً، ويفهم من عدم التصور التصور مع وجود ما يقتضيه وكذلك الذهول يقرب منه قيل : وسببه عدم استثبات التصور حيرة ودهشاً، قال الله تعالى : يوم ترونها تذهل كل مرضعة عما أرضعت [ الحج : ٢]، فهو قسم من السهو والجهل البسيط بعد العلم يسمى نسيانا . وقد فرق بين السهو والنسيان بأن الأول : زوال الصورة عن المدركة مع بقائها في الحافظة، والثاني : زوالها عنهما معاً، فيحتاج حينئذ
The fourth objective: simple ignorance. 'Ignorance' is said of the compound, which we mentioned, and it is also said of the simple, which is the absence of knowledge concerning that which is apt to be knowledgeable, so it is not a contrary of knowledge, but rather an opposite to it as the absence to the possession. And similar to it, i.e., to simple ignorance, is oversight, and it is like simple ignorance, its cause is the instability of the concept, i.e., knowledge whether conceptual or assertoric, for when the concept is not firmly established and not settled, it is liable to disappear, so it is established once and disappears another time, and another concept is established in its place, so one is confused with the other in an unstable confusion, until when the forgetful one is alerted by the slightest alert, he becomes alert and the first concept returns to him. And likewise, heedlessness is close to ignorance as well, and it is understood from 'absence of concept' the concept with the existence of what necessitates it. And likewise, distraction is close to it; it was said: its cause is the instability of the concept due to confusion and bewilderment. Allah Most High said: 'On the day you see it, every nursing mother will forget what she nursed' [al-Hajj: 2]; thus it is a type of oversight. And simple ignorance after knowledge is called forgetfulness. And a distinction was made between oversight and forgetfulness: the first is the removal of the image from the perceiver while it remains in the memory, and the second is its removal from both together; then one needs...
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage defines simple ignorance ('al-jahl al-basit') and contrasts it with compound ignorance. Simple ignorance is not a contrary (did) of knowledge but a privative opposite ('adam al-malaka'), meaning the absence of knowledge from a subject that is capable of having it. It then discusses related concepts: sahu (oversight) – a type of simple ignorance caused by instability of the mental concept (tasawwur or tasdiq); when the concept is not firmly fixed, it vacillates and can be replaced by another, causing confusion until a slight reminder brings it back. Ghafla (heedlessness) is also close to ignorance; it involves absence of attention despite the presence of the concept's requirements. Dhuhul (distraction) is similar, often caused by confusion or bewilderment as exemplified by the Quranic verse about mothers forgetting their infants on the Day of Judgment. Finally, the passage distinguishes sahu from nisyan (forgetfulness): sahu involves loss of the image from the active perceiver while it remains in the memory, whereas nisyan involves loss from both the perceiver and the memory. The passage ends incompletely, but the main definitions and distinctions are clear.

العدم

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Simple ignorance is a direct contrary (ḍidd) of knowledge.
Correction: The passage explicitly says it is not a contrary but a privative opposite, like the absence of sight in one who is capable of sight.

Key Takeaway
Simple ignorance is the absence of knowledge from someone capable of it, and it is distinguished from oversight, heedlessness, distraction, and forgetfulness by the cause and persistence of the mental image.

Simple Ignorance: Absence of Knowledge, Not a Contrary
Defines simple ignorance and its relation to knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of simple ignorance (al-jahl al-basīṭ) and why it is considered a privation (ʿadam) opposite of knowledge rather than a contrary (ḍidd).

Core Idea
Simple ignorance is not the opposite of knowledge, but its absence in a capable subject.

This lesson introduces a key distinction in epistemology: simple ignorance is not a positive quality opposing knowledge, but rather the mere lack of it, like blindness to sight. The author, al-Sanūsī, clarifies this by contrasting simple ignorance with compound ignorance and by specifying its relation to knowledge as privation versus possession. Let us read the passage carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of simple ignorance
Locate the phrase defining simple ignorance: 'absence of knowledge concerning that which is apt to be knowing.' Notice the condition that it applies only to beings capable of knowledge.
2
Understand the opposition type
The author explicitly says it is not a contrary (ḍidd) but a privation-possession opposite. Contrast this with opposites like black and white.
3
Differentiate from compound ignorance
Compound ignorance was previously defined as false belief. Simple ignorance is mere absence. Keep this distinction in mind.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجهل يقال للمركب، وهو ما ذكرناه و يقال أيضاً للبسيط، وهو عدم العلم عما من شأنه أن يكون عالماً ، فلا يكون ضداً للعلم، بل مقابلاً له مقابلة العدم للملكة ويقرب منه أي من الجهل البسيط السهو وهو كان جهل بسيط سببه عدم استثبات التصور أي العلم تصورياً كان أو تصديقياً فإنه إذا لم يتمكن التصور، ولم يتقرر كان في معرض الزوال فيثبت مرة ويزول أخرى، ويثبت بدله تصور آخر فيشتبه أحدهما بالآخر اشتباهاً غير مستقر حتى إذا نبه الساهي أدنى تنبيه تنبه وعاد إليه التصور الأول وكذلك الغفلة تقرب من الجهل أيضاً، ويفهم من عدم التصور التصور مع وجود ما يقتضيه وكذلك الذهول يقرب منه قيل : وسببه عدم استثبات التصور حيرة ودهشاً، قال الله تعالى : يوم ترونها تذهل كل مرضعة عما أرضعت الله [ الحج : ٢]، فهو قسم من السهو والجهل البسيط بعد العلم يسمى نسيانا .
Ignorance (al-jahl) is said of compound [ignorance] (al-murakkab) – which we have mentioned – and is also said of simple [ignorance] (al-basīṭ), which is the absence of knowledge (ʿadam al-ʿilm) concerning that which is apt to be knowing (ʿammā min shaʾnihi an yakūna ʿāliman). Thus it is not a contrary (ḍidd) of knowledge, but rather an opposite (muqābil) to it as the opposite of privation (al-ʿadam) to possession (al-milka). And close to it – i.e., to simple ignorance – is sahw (sahw), which is a simple ignorance whose cause is the lack of stabilization of the concept (tasawwur) – i.e., knowledge, whether conceptual or assentive – for when the concept is not firmly established and is not settled, it is subject to vanishing; so it becomes fixed at one time and vanishes at another, and another concept becomes fixed in its place, so they are confused with each other in an unstable confusion. So when the inattentive one (al-sāhī) is alerted with the slightest alert, he becomes alert and the first concept returns to him. Similarly, forgetfulness (al-ghafla) is also close to ignorance; and it is understood from non-conception that conception exists with what necessitates it. And likewise, heedlessness (al-dhuhūl) is close to it. It is said: its cause is the lack of stabilization of the concept due to confusion and bewilderment. Allah the Exalted said: 'On the day you see it, every nursing mother will neglect (tadhhalu) what she nursed' [al-Ḥajj:2]. So it is a type of sahw. And simple ignorance after knowledge is called forgetfulness (nisyān).
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage defines simple ignorance (al-jahl al-basīṭ) and distinguishes it from compound ignorance (al-jahl al-murakkab). Compound ignorance was previously defined as the belief that something is other than it is; here the focus is on simple ignorance, which is the absence of knowledge in a being that is capable of having knowledge. The author emphasizes that simple ignorance is not a contrary (ḍidd) of knowledge, but rather an opposite of the type privation vs. possession (muqābala al-ʿadam li al-milka). This means knowledge is a positive quality, and its absence in a capable subject is the mere lack of that quality, not a positive opposite. The passage then lists phenomena that approximate simple ignorance: sahw (inadvertence), ghafla (forgetfulness as in lack of attention), dhuhūl (heedlessness), and nisyān (forgetting after knowledge). For sahw, the cause is the lack of stabilization of the concept (tasawwur) in the mind, so that it is prone to disappear and be replaced by another concept, leading to unstable confusion. Once alerted, the original concept returns. Ghafla is also close to ignorance, and dhuhūl is similar, with its cause being lack of stabilization due to confusion (ḥayra) and bewilderment (dahash), citing Qur'an 22:2. Finally, simple ignorance that occurs after knowledge is specifically called nisyān (forgetting).

Key Terms
الجهل البسيط
الجهل المركب
مقابلة العدم للملكة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think simple ignorance is a positive quality that opposes knowledge, like darkness opposes light.
Correction: The author clarifies it is merely the absence of knowledge, not a positive opposite. It is like blindness, which is the lack of sight, not a quality in itself.

Checkpoint
How is simple ignorance (jahl basit) opposed to knowledge according to this passage?
A) As a contrary (ḍidd)
B) As a privation vs. possession (muqābala al-ʿadam li al-milka)
C) As a contradictory (tanāquḍ)
D) As a relational opposite (muqābala al-ḍiddayn)

Key Takeaway
Simple ignorance is the absence of knowledge in a capable being, and it opposes knowledge as a privation opposes possession.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Approximations of Simple Ignorance: Sahw, Ghafla, Dhuhul, and Nisyan
Explains four states proximate to simple ignorance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four phenomena that approximate simple ignorance: sahw, ghafla, dhuhul, and nisyan, and how each relates to the concept of ignorance.

Core Idea
Sahw, ghafla, dhuhul, and nisyan are states close to simple ignorance, each arising from instability of knowledge.

After defining simple ignorance, the author turns to four related states that resemble it: inadvertence (sahw), forgetfulness (ghafla), heedlessness (dhuhul), and forgetting after knowledge (nisyan). These are not full-blown ignorance but share the characteristic of a lack or instability of knowledge. The passage explains each state and its cause, grounding them in the broader framework of simple ignorance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four approximations
The passage lists sahw, ghafla, dhuhul, and nisyan. Read through each definition and note how they are similar to simple ignorance.
2
Understand sahw (inadvertence)
Sahw is a simple ignorance caused by lack of stabilization of the concept. When a concept is not firmly established, it can be replaced by another, leading to confusion. The sahw is resolved with a slight alert.
3
Grasp ghafla and dhuhul
Ghafla involves having a concept but not attending to it. Dhuhul is similar, caused by confusion and bewilderment, supported by Quranic verse.
4
Note nisyan (forgetting)
Nisyan is specifically simple ignorance that occurs after previously having knowledge. It is not mentioned as having a separate cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجهل يقال للمركب، وهو ما ذكرناه و يقال أيضاً للبسيط، وهو عدم العلم عما من شأنه أن يكون عالماً ، فلا يكون ضداً للعلم، بل مقابلاً له مقابلة العدم للملكة ويقرب منه أي من الجهل البسيط السهو وهو كان جهل بسيط سببه عدم استثبات التصور أي العلم تصورياً كان أو تصديقياً فإنه إذا لم يتمكن التصور، ولم يتقرر كان في معرض الزوال فيثبت مرة ويزول أخرى، ويثبت بدله تصور آخر فيشتبه أحدهما بالآخر اشتباهاً غير مستقر حتى إذا نبه الساهي أدنى تنبيه تنبه وعاد إليه التصور الأول وكذلك الغفلة تقرب من الجهل أيضاً، ويفهم من عدم التصور التصور مع وجود ما يقتضيه وكذلك الذهول يقرب منه قيل : وسببه عدم استثبات التصور حيرة ودهشاً، قال الله تعالى : يوم ترونها تذهل كل مرضعة عما أرضعت الله [ الحج : ٢]، فهو قسم من السهو والجهل البسيط بعد العلم يسمى نسيانا .
Ignorance (al-jahl) is said of compound [ignorance] (al-murakkab) – which we have mentioned – and is also said of simple [ignorance] (al-basīṭ), which is the absence of knowledge (ʿadam al-ʿilm) concerning that which is apt to be knowing (ʿammā min shaʾnihi an yakūna ʿāliman). Thus it is not a contrary (ḍidd) of knowledge, but rather an opposite (muqābil) to it as the opposite of privation (al-ʿadam) to possession (al-milka). And close to it – i.e., to simple ignorance – is sahw (sahw), which is a simple ignorance whose cause is the lack of stabilization of the concept (tasawwur) – i.e., knowledge, whether conceptual or assentive – for when the concept is not firmly established and is not settled, it is subject to vanishing; so it becomes fixed at one time and vanishes at another, and another concept becomes fixed in its place, so they are confused with each other in an unstable confusion. So when the inattentive one (al-sāhī) is alerted with the slightest alert, he becomes alert and the first concept returns to him. Similarly, forgetfulness (al-ghafla) is also close to ignorance; and it is understood from non-conception that conception exists with what necessitates it. And likewise, heedlessness (al-dhuhūl) is close to it. It is said: its cause is the lack of stabilization of the concept due to confusion and bewilderment. Allah the Exalted said: 'On the day you see it, every nursing mother will neglect (tadhhalu) what she nursed' [al-Ḥajj:2]. So it is a type of sahw. And simple ignorance after knowledge is called forgetfulness (nisyān).
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage defines simple ignorance (al-jahl al-basīṭ) and distinguishes it from compound ignorance (al-jahl al-murakkab). Compound ignorance was previously defined as the belief that something is other than it is; here the focus is on simple ignorance, which is the absence of knowledge in a being that is capable of having knowledge. The author emphasizes that simple ignorance is not a contrary (ḍidd) of knowledge, but rather an opposite of the type privation vs. possession (muqābala al-ʿadam li al-milka). This means knowledge is a positive quality, and its absence in a capable subject is the mere lack of that quality, not a positive opposite. The passage then lists phenomena that approximate simple ignorance: sahw (inadvertence), ghafla (forgetfulness as in lack of attention), dhuhūl (heedlessness), and nisyān (forgetting after knowledge). For sahw, the cause is the lack of stabilization of the concept (tasawwur) in the mind, so that it is prone to disappear and be replaced by another concept, leading to unstable confusion. Once alerted, the original concept returns. Ghafla is also close to ignorance, and dhuhūl is similar, with its cause being lack of stabilization due to confusion (ḥayra) and bewilderment (dahash), citing Qur'an 22:2. Finally, simple ignorance that occurs after knowledge is specifically called nisyān (forgetting).

Key Terms
السهو
الغفلة
الذهول
النسيان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the cause of sahw (inadvertence) according to this passage?
A) Lack of stabilization of the concept
B) Complete absence of knowledge
C) Presence of false belief
D) Quranic neglect

Key Takeaway
Sahw, ghafla, dhuhul, and nisyan are all states where knowledge is unstable or absent, approximating simple ignorance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have likely experienced sahw when reciting Quran: you recite mechanically but your mind is elsewhere. Have you ever noticed that a slight alert brings you back? What does this teach you about the instability of your own knowledge and the need for constant awareness (muraqaba)? How will you train yourself to reduce sahw in your acts of worship starting today?
?
Ghafla is described as having knowledge but not attending to it. Think of a command of Allah you know but neglect. Does knowing the ruling excuse you on the Day of Judgment? What one action will you take this week to move from ghafla to active remembrance?
?
Nisyan is simple ignorance after knowledge. What is one piece of Islamic knowledge you once knew well but have forgotten? Is it acceptable to leave it forgotten? What specific step will you take to revive it?

Taqlid, Jahl Basit, and the Distinction of Matter vs Truth
Answers an objection about taqlid being an example of jahl basit despite earlier definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why taqlid is not a simple example of jahl basit, and how the distinction between matter (madda) and truth (sidq) resolves the objection.

Core Idea
Taqlid has the matter of ignorance, but not its truth.

This passage answers a subtle objection about taqlid (blind acceptance) being classified as simple ignorance. The author earlier defined simple ignorance as the privative opposite of knowledge, but taqlid seems to be a positive belief. How can it be a mere absence? The response introduces a crucial distinction between the material of a concept and its truth predicate.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector notes that taqlid was said to be not knowledge, thus simple ignorance, and earlier examples included taqlid for common people. So how can simple ignorance be a privative opposite?
Understanding the objection is key.
2
Examine the answer's distinction
The author distinguishes between 'existence in the matter' (al-wujud fi al-madda) and 'truth predication' (al-sidq). Simple ignorance exists in the matter of taqlid but is not truly predicated on it.
This is a fine logical point.
3
Apply the distinction
Consider why taqlid is not simply an absence: it involves a belief, so it is not a pure privation. The answer preserves the general definition while resolving the apparent counterexample.
This shows the precision of kalam reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (مقابلة العدم للملكة إن قلت : قد سبق في تحقيق تعريف العلم أن التقليد ليس بعلم إلا مجازاً فهو من قبيل الجهل البسيط، وقد ذكر اتفاق الكل على كون بعض أفراده مثلاً للعام، فكيف يكون الجهل البسيط مطلقاً مقابلاً للعلم تقابل العدم للملكة؟ قلت : اللازم مما مر وجود الجهل البسيط في مادة التقليد لا صدقه عليه فلا محظور.
His saying: (Opposition of privation to possession). If you say: It has preceded in the verification of the definition of knowledge that taqlīd is not knowledge except metaphorically, so it is of the category of simple ignorance, and it has been mentioned that all agree that some of its individuals are an example for the common, so how can simple ignorance absolutely be an opposite of knowledge as the opposition of privation to possession? I say: What follows from what has passed is the existence of simple ignorance in the matter (mādda) of taqlīd, not its truth (ṣidq) upon it; so there is no prohibition.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the definition of simple ignorance as an opposite of knowledge in the mode of privation vs. possession. The objector points out that earlier it was established that taqlīd (blind acceptance) is not knowledge except metaphorically, and thus it falls under simple ignorance. Moreover, it was agreed that some instances of taqlīd serve as examples for the common people. So, if simple ignorance is simply the absence of knowledge in a capable subject, how can it be an opposite of knowledge? This seems to clash because taqlīd involves belief, not just absence. The answer (qultu) resolves this by distinguishing between the matter (mādda) and the truth (ṣidq) of the concept. Simple ignorance exists in the material of taqlīd – i.e., taqlīd involves a lack of knowledge in a certain sense – but simple ignorance is not truly predicated (ṣādiq) upon taqlīd. This distinction preserves the general rule that simple ignorance is a privative opposite, while taqlīd, as a belief state, is not simply an absence but a positive belief, even if it is not genuine knowledge.

Key Terms
مقابلة العدم للملكة
التقليد
المادة
الصدق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Taqlid is a clear example of jahl basit without qualification.
Correction: Taqlid has the matter of ignorance but not its truth; it is not simply the absence of knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the answer in this passage, how is taqlīd related to simple ignorance?
A) Taqlīd is identical to simple ignorance
B) Simple ignorance is truly predicated on taqlīd
C) Simple ignorance exists in the matter of taqlīd but is not truly predicated on it
D) Taqlīd is not related to simple ignorance at all

Key Takeaway
Taqlid has the matter of simple ignorance but not its truth, preserving the definition of jahl basit as a privative opposite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that even a belief you hold can be 'ignorance in matter'—meaning it has the substance of ignorance even if you think it is true. Examine your own most deeply held beliefs: which of them are based on personal verification, and which have you simply accepted from others (taqlid)? Are you willing to subject each one to the test of evidence?
?
The distinction between matter and truth forces you to confront the foundation of your faith. Is your īmān merely inherited and imitative, or have you sought the truth that dispels every shadow of ignorance? Commit today to verify one core Islamic belief—such as the existence of God—using rational and textual proofs, so that your belief moves from taqlid to true knowledge.

Ash'ari on Sensory Perceptions as Knowledge
States Ash'ari's view that sensory perceptions are knowledge, with two glosses on terminology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ash'ari's classification of sensory perceptions as knowledge and the accompanying debate over terminology.

Core Idea
For Ash'ari, seeing and hearing are acts of knowledge, not mere sensation.

In this lesson, we examine the Fifth Objective of a Kalam work, where al-Ash'ari asserts that the perceptions of the five external senses are actually knowledge of their objects. This position is illustrated with examples and contrasted with animal perception. The passage also includes a gloss that addresses the tension between this technical definition and everyday language, offering a nuanced defense.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the full translation to grasp the author's flow.
Notice the structure: main thesis, examples, then two glosses.
2
Identify Ash'ari's claim
Find the sentence stating that perceptions of the five senses are knowledge of their objects. Note how hearing is knowledge of sounds, sight of visible things, etc.
3
Understand the means analogy
The passage likens sensory perception to intuition, self-evidence, and speculation as means of obtaining knowledge. Why are senses placed alongside these cognitive tools?
This shows sensory knowledge is not passive but active acquisition.
4
Examine the human-animal distinction
The first gloss restricts this to humans; animal perceptions are not called knowledge. Find the reason and the implication for the term 'possessors of knowledge'.
This distinction is crucial for the later terminology debate.
5
Analyze the terminology gloss
Two statements appear about whether calling sensation 'knowledge' contradicts custom and language. Compare them: one dismisses the objection, the other acknowledges the conflict but affirms the technical usage.
This reveals a nuanced stance: technical definition vs. common parlance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الخامس : إدراكات الحواس الظاهرة ] (إدراكات الحواس الخمس الظاهرة ( عند الشيخ ) الأشعري ( علم بمتعلقاتها فالسمع أي الإدراك بالسامعة (علم بالمسموعات والابصار) أي الإدراك بالباصرة (علم بالمبصرات ) وكذلك الحال في الإدراك باللامسة والذائقة والشامة، فهذه الحواس وسائل إلى تلك العلوم الحاصلة باستعمالها كالوجدان والبديهة والنظر التي قوله: ( إدراك الحواس الخمس (الظاهرة أي للإنسان بخلاف الحيوانات فإن إدراكها بالحواس ليس بعلم، ولذا لا يقال لها : أولو العلم فما قيل أن كون الاحساس من العلم يخالف العرف واللغة ليس بشيء. قوله: (علم) بمتعلقاتها قد سبق الإشارة إلى أن الحق أن إطلاق العلم على الإحساس مخالف للعرف واللغة فإنه فيهما اسم لغيره من الإدراكات ولذا لا يعد البهائم من أولى العلم في شيء منهما .
The Fifth Objective: Perceptions of the External Senses. (The perceptions of the five external senses, according to the Shaykh al-Ashʿarī, are knowledge (ʿilm) of their objects: hearing – i.e., perception by the ear – is knowledge of sounds; sight – i.e., perception by the eye – is knowledge of visible things; and similarly for perception by touch, taste, and smell. So these senses are means to those knowledges that are obtained by using them, like intuition (al-wijdān), self-evidence (al-badīha), and speculation (al-naẓar) which ... His saying: (The perception of the five senses (the external) i.e., for man, unlike animals, for their perception by the senses is not knowledge; therefore they are not called possessors of knowledge (ulū al-ʿilm). So what is said that sensation being [considered] knowledge contradicts custom and language is nothing. His saying: (Knowledge) of their objects: it has already been indicated that the truth is that applying the term 'knowledge' to sensation is contrary to custom and language, for in both it is a name for other perceptions, and therefore animals are not counted among the possessors of knowledge in either of them.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage presents the Fifth Objective (al-maqṣad al-khāmis) concerning the perceptions of the external senses. It states the position of Shaykh Abū al-Ḥasan al-Ashʿarī that the perceptions of the five external senses – hearing, sight, touch, taste, and smell – are considered knowledge (ʿilm) of their respective objects. These senses are means to obtain that knowledge, similar to intuition (wijdān), self-evidence (badīha), and speculation (naẓar). The first gloss clarifies that this applies only to humans, not animals; animal perceptions are not called knowledge, which is why animals are not called 'possessors of knowledge' (ulū al-ʿilm). It then dismisses the objection that calling sensation 'knowledge' contradicts common usage (ʿurf) and language (lugha) as unfounded. However, the second gloss offers a nuance: it acknowledges that the correct view is that applying the term 'knowledge' to sensation is indeed contrary to common usage and language, because in both, 'knowledge' is a name for other types of perceptions, and animals are not considered possessors of knowledge. This indicates a tension: while Ashʿarī technically classifies sensory perceptions as knowledge, the commentator notes that everyday language reserves 'knowledge' for higher cognitive acts. The passage thus highlights the technical theological definition versus conventional usage.

الأشعري

أولو العلم

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Ash'ari's view was universally accepted without any terminological controversy.
Correction: The passage shows a clear tension: the second gloss admits that calling sensation 'knowledge' goes against normal custom and language, indicating a technical usage that deliberately differs from everyday speech.

Checkpoint

A) They are not knowledge but mere sensations
B) They are knowledge of their objects
C) They are knowledge only for animals
D) They are a form of speculation (naẓar)

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari defines sensory perceptions as knowledge for humans, even though this technical usage may differ from everyday language.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have senses that allow you to see, hear, and feel the world. Do you ever pause to recognise that these very sensations are a form of knowledge that points you to your Creator? When you look at a sunset tonight, will you treat it as mere colour, or as a sign from Allah that demands your gratitude and reflection?
?
The passage distinguishes humans from animals: animals perceive but are not called 'possessors of knowledge'. What makes your sensory experience different? Is your heart truly aware that every sight and sound is a gift meant to increase your knowledge of Allah, or do you live like an animal, just sensing without reflecting?
?
Ashʿarī’s view may differ from how you normally speak about knowledge. But in your daily life, do you reduce your faith to familiar words and customs, or do you strive for a direct, personal knowledge of Allah through His signs? What will you do today to move from mere sensation to real, transformative knowledge?
