Are Sense Perceptions Knowledge? The Debate
Taftazani presents the majority's objection and the shaykh's answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the majority of mutakallimun objected to considering sense perceptions as knowledge, and how the shaykh defended their status as a type of knowledge despite apparent differences.

Core Idea
Sense perceptions are a valid type of knowledge, differing only in type or identity.

In this passage, Taftazani and the shaykh debate whether sense perceptions count as knowledge. The majority argues that there is a felt difference between knowing an object and perceiving it, which seems to rule out sense perceptions as knowledge. The shaykh responds with a nuanced argument that this difference does not preclude sense perceptions from being a kind of knowledge—they can differ by type or by individual identity while still belonging to the category of knowledge. This lesson unpacks their exchange and its implications for how we define knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then the translation. Notice the structure: objection then answer.
The passage uses technical terms like haqiqa jinsiyya and haqiqa naw'iyya.
2
Understand the majority's objection
Identify the example given (color) and the reasoning that if sight were knowledge there would be no difference between knowing and seeing. Why does this seem persuasive?
The objection relies on a necessary felt difference.
3
Analyze the shaykh's reply
The shaykh does not deny the felt difference but reinterprets it. He says sense perceptions are knowledge that differs either by type (naw') or by identity (huwiyya). Understand the two scenarios.
This is a key move in Islamic epistemology.
4
Connect to broader themes
Reflect on how this debate relates to the definition of knowledge and the role of sense perception in acquiring it. How does the shaykh's answer preserve the unity of knowledge?
This links to the earlier definitions of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثاني - المقصد الخامس : إدراكات الحواس الظاهرة يتوسل بها إلى العلوم المستندة إليها، وخالفه فيه الجمهور من المتكلمين. فإنا إذا علمنا شيئاً كاللون مثلاً علماً تاماً، ثم رأيناه فإنا نجد بين الحالتين فرقاً ضرورياً، ونعلم أنَّ الحالة الثانية مخالفة للحالة الأولى بلا شبهة، ولو كان الإبصار علماً بالمبصر لم يكن هناك فرق وهكذا نجد الفرق بين العلم بهذا الصوت وسماعه وبين العلم بهذا الطعم وذوقه وبين العلم بهذه الرائحة وشمها. وله أي للشيخ أن يجيب بأن ذلك الفرق الوجداني لا يمنع كونه أي كون إدراك الحواس علماً مخالفاً لسائر العلوم المستندة إلى غير الحواس مخالفة إما بالنوع أو بالهوية، فيكون العلم على الأول حقيقة جنسية مشتملة على حقائق مختلفة منها إدراك الحواس، وعلى الثاني حقيقة نوعية متناولة لأفراد متخالفة بالهويات. قوله: (كالوجدان والبديهة إلخ) يعني كما أن هذه الثلاثة طرق ووسائل كذلك الحواس طرق والانكشاف في الجميع إنما هو للنفس فكون الحاصل بتلك الطرق علماً دون الحاصل بالحواس تحكم. قوله: (وللشيخ أن يجيب إلخ) خلاصته أن اختلاف أمرين في ذاتي أو عارض لا ينافي الاتفاق في الحقيقة الجنسية أو النوعية.
The second type - the fifth purpose: perceptions of the external senses are used as means to the sciences that are based on them, and the majority of the mutakallimun disagreed with him on that. For if we know something, like color for example, with complete knowledge, then we see it, we find a necessary difference between the two states, and we know that the second state is different from the first without doubt. If sight were knowledge of the visible object, there would be no difference. Similarly we find a difference between knowledge of this sound and hearing it, between knowledge of this taste and tasting it, and between knowledge of this smell and smelling it. And he, meaning the shaykh, may answer that that felt difference does not prevent it, i.e., the perception of the senses, from being knowledge that differs from other sciences that are not based on the senses, either in type (al-naw') or in identity (al-huwiyya). On the first view, knowledge is a generic reality (haqiqa jinsiyya) comprising different realities including sense perception. On the second view, it is a specific reality (haqiqa naw'iyya) encompassing individuals that differ in identities. His statement: (like intuition and self-evidence etc.) means that just as these three are paths and means, so too the senses are paths, and the unveiling in all of them is only to the soul; so deeming what is obtained by those paths as knowledge without deeming what is obtained by the senses as knowledge is arbitrary. His statement: (and the shaykh may answer etc.) its summary is that the difference between two things in an essential or accidental attribute does not negate agreement in generic or specific reality.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage presents the beginning of the fifth purpose (al-maqsad al-khamis) dealing with sense perceptions. It states that the majority of the mutakallimun disagreed with the shaykh's view that sense perceptions are a means to knowledge. Their objection is based on a necessary difference we find between knowing an object and perceiving it through the senses. For example, knowing a color intellectually is different from seeing it; similarly for sound, taste, smell. If sense perception were identical to knowledge, there would be no such difference. The shaykh responds that this felt difference does not prevent sense perception from being a kind of knowledge. It can differ from other knowledge either in type (naw') or in identity (huwiyya). If by type, then knowledge is a generic concept encompassing various specific realities including sense perception; if by identity, then knowledge is a specific concept with individuals differing in their individual identities. The commentary then explains: the reference to intuition and self-evidence indicates that all these are paths to the soul's disclosure; denying the status of knowledge to sense perceptions is arbitrary. The second commentary summarises: difference in essential or accidental attributes does not preclude agreement in a common generic or specific reality.

Key Terms
الحواس الظاهرة
العلم
الإبصار
الفرق الوجداني
النوع
الهوية
حقيقة جنسية
حقيقة نوعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the shaykh denies the evident difference between knowing and perceiving.
Correction: The shaykh does not deny the felt difference; he reinterprets it as consistent with sense perceptions being a type of knowledge, differing either in type or identity.

Checkpoint
What is the shaykh's main response to the majority's objection that sense perception differs from knowledge?
A) He denies the felt difference exists.
B) He argues that the difference is only conceptual and not real.
C) He says the difference does not prevent sense perception from being knowledge, either by type or identity.
D) He agrees that sense perception is not knowledge but calls it something else.

Key Takeaway
The shaykh defends sense perception as a type of knowledge by showing that the observed difference does not preclude it from belonging to the same genus or species as other knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you taste a familiar flavor or hear a sound, do you instinctively treat that experience as 'just a sensation' rather than genuine knowledge? How would your daily life change if you began to see every sensory perception as a real form of knowing, as the shaykh argues?
?
The passage reveals that even scholars debated the very definition of knowledge. How does that humility affect your own certainty about what you claim to know? Are there areas in your life where you insist on absolute knowledge when the scholars themselves admitted ambiguity?
?
The shaykh offers two possible explanations for the difference between knowing and sensing. Which explanation—difference by type or by identity—resonates more with your own experience? Why does it matter which one is correct for how you relate to your own perceptions?

The Philosophers’ View on Intellectual Forms
Explains the philosophers' view on intellectual forms and their universality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' distinction between external and intellectual forms and their explanation of universality through common meaning.

Core Idea
Intellectual forms are universal meanings abstracted from all individuating features.

This lesson presents the philosophers' doctrine on intellectual forms, contrasting them with external forms. You'll learn why intellectual forms are considered universal and how the concept of 'humanity' exemplifies this. The passage also clarifies the difference between homonymy and commonality in meaning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the overall argument about intellectual forms and their universality.
The passage contains both a general statement and a detailed example.
2
Identify the two key differences
Note the two distinctions: external forms are sensible and particular, while intellectual forms are not sensible and universal.
3
Analyze the example of 'humanity'
See how the philosophers explain universality through the name 'human' – it applies by a common meaning, not by homonymy.
This illustrates the concept of ishtirak ma'nawi (commonality in meaning).
4
Understand the exclusion of individuators
Recognize that the common meaning excludes all individuating attributes (mushakhkhasat), otherwise it wouldn't be common to all individuals.

النوع الثاني - المقصد السادس : قول الحكماء في الصور العقلية الصور الخارجية فإنَّها واجبة الزوال عن المادة العنصرية لاستحالة بقاء قواها أبداً وإذا زالت احتيج في استرجاعها إلى مثل السبب الأول، ومن الفرق بينهما أنَّ الصور الخارجية قد تكون محسوسة بالحواس الظاهرة بخلاف الصور العقلية، ومنها أنَّ الصور العقلية كلية بخلاف الخارجية (ثم ) إنهم (ذكروا في معنى كون صورة (الإنسانية) المعقولة (أمراً كلياً أمرين الأول : اسم الإنسان ) مثلاً ( لإفراده ليس باشتراك اللفظ ضرورة مثل : اشتراك لفظ العين بين معانيه التي وضع لفظه بإزاء كل منها على حدة، بل هو ) أي مدلول اسم الإنسان ( معنى مشترك ) بين إفراده وإطلاقه عليها باعتبار ذلك المعنى، وهذا هو الذي يسمى اشتراكاً معنوياً ( ولا يدخل فيه) أي في ذلك المعنى المشترك ( المشخصات ) التي يمتاز بها أفراد بعضها عن بعض، ( وإلا لم يكن ذلك المعنى مشتركاً بين جميع أفراده، بل المشخصات كلها خارجة عنه ( فالنفس) الناطقة ( إذ استحضرت صورة الإنسانية ) أي صورة ذلك المعنى المشترك ( مجردة عن المشخصات ) التي هي عوارض غريبة ولواحق خارجية
The second type - the sixth topic: The statement of the philosophers regarding intellectual forms. External forms are necessarily passing away from elemental matter due to the impossibility of the permanence of their powers forever, and when they pass away, one needs for their retrieval something like the first cause. And among the differences between them is that external forms may be perceived by the external senses, unlike intellectual forms, and among them is that intellectual forms are universal, unlike external forms. Then they mentioned concerning the meaning of the intelligible form of 'humanity' being a universal matter two things: the first: the name 'human' for its individuals is not by homonymy, necessarily, like the homonymy of the word 'ayn among its meanings for which the word was placed in exchange for each of them separately, but it—i.e., the denotation of the name 'human'—is a common meaning among its individuals, and its application to them is by virtue of that meaning, and this is what is called commonality in meaning. And the individuators by which individuals are distinguished from each other do not enter into it, otherwise that meaning would not be common among all its individuals; rather, all individuators are outside it. So the rational soul, when it recalls the image of humanity—i.e., the image of that common meaning—stripped of the individuators that are accidental attributes and external concomitants.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' doctrine on intellectual forms and defines their key characteristics. It begins by stating that external forms are necessarily perishable from elemental matter because their powers cannot last forever; when they vanish, they require a new act of the First Cause for recovery. Then two differences between external and intellectual forms are given: first, external forms can be sensed by the external senses, while intellectual forms cannot; second, intellectual forms are universal, whereas external forms are particular. The passage then explains the meaning of universality using the example of the concept 'humanity'. The philosophers say that the name 'human' applies to its individuals not by homonymy (like the word 'ayn meaning eye, spring, etc.), but by a common meaning shared by all individuals—this is called 'commonality in meaning' (ishtirak ma'nawi). This common meaning excludes all individuating characteristics (mushakhkhasat), because if it included them, it would not be common to all. The rational soul, when it apprehends the image of humanity, does so stripped of these individuating accidents and external concomitants. Thus the intellectual form of humanity is a universal concept, not tied to any particular external individual.

Key Terms
الصور العقلية
الصور الخارجية
المادة العنصرية
الاشتراك المعنوي
المشخصات
النفس الناطقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Intellectual forms exist as separate Platonic entities independent of the mind.
Correction: The passage clarifies that intellectual forms are abstract meanings in the mind, not independently existing entities.

Checkpoint
What is the key difference between external forms and intellectual forms according to the philosophers in this passage?
A) External forms are eternal, intellectual forms are temporal.
B) External forms are particular and perceptible, intellectual forms are universal and abstract.
C) Intellectual forms are perceived by the senses, external forms are intellectual.
D) Both are equally universal.

Key Takeaway
Intellectual forms are universal meanings abstracted from all particularizing features, not Platonically real entities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at another person, do you see them first as a human being sharing the same common meaning of humanity, or do you focus on their individualizing features (race, appearance, status) and let those define your attitude? This lesson shows that the rational soul abstracts from such accidents. How can you train yourself to see the universal humanity in everyone you meet, starting today?
?
The philosophers say external forms are passing away and require a first cause for retrieval. Your body and everything you own are external forms—temporary and dependent on Allah. Does this reality shape how you prioritize your life? What is one attachment to a worldly external form that you need to release, and what concrete step will you take this week to detach from it?
?
You have a universal essence (humanity) stripped of particulars. If you were to remove from your self-concept all accidental traits (wealth, job, nationality), what remains? That core is your true self before Allah. How does that awareness change the way you evaluate your own worth and the worth of others? Write a specific commitment to stop judging yourself or others by those passing accidents.

What Is Kalam?
intermediate
6 min
Universality as Correspondence with a Shadowy Existence
Defines kulliyya as a correspondence with a shadowy essence and extends it to other universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Sanusi defines universality (kulliyya) as a correspondence (mutabaqa) with a shadowy existence and how this applies to real types and other universals.

Core Idea
Universality is a shadowy correspondence, not a real attribute of external things.

Before we can understand how universality relates to mental forms, al-Sanusi establishes what universality itself means. He presents a precise definition: it is not a straightforward correspondence but one tied to a shadowy essence that lacks independent existence. This subtle definition will ground the later discussion on whether universality genuinely attaches to mental forms or only to the objects known through them.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of kulliyya
Read the Arabic passage where al-Sanusi defines kulliyya as 'mutabaqa mutlaqan' then refines it to 'mutabaqat dhat mithaliyya...'. Notice the shift from absolute correspondence to a qualified one.
The term 'mutabaqa' is a technical term for the relation of a universal to its instances.
2
Distinguish real types from other universals
Al-Sanusi explains that this definition is clearest for 'anwa' haqiqiyya' (real types). For other universals, we must either measure them to their conceptual individuals or treat non-common meanings as particularizers. Understand why this extension is needed.
This shows that the definition is not one-size-fits-all; it requires adaptation.
3
Reflect on 'shadowy existence'
The phrase 'dhat mithaliyya ghayr muta'assila fi al-wujud' is crucial. Ask: What does it mean for an essence to be a shadow and not rooted in existence? Consider how this fits with the idea that universals are mind-dependent.
This connects to later debates about mental existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثاني - المقصد السادس : قول الحكماء في الصور العقلية
بل عن الكلية عبارة عن المطابقة مطلقاً بل مطابقة ذات مثالية غير متأصلة في الوجود لما هي ظل لها، واعلم أنَّ ما ذكر في تصوير المطابقة التي هي معنى الكلية، إنما يظهر في الكليات التي هي أنواع حقيقية، فإذا أريد أجزاؤه في سائر الكليات، قيست إلى حصصها التي هي أفرادها الاعتبارية، فإنها أنواع حقيقية بالقياس إليها، أو جعل ما عدا المعنى المشترك بين أفرادها بمنزلة المشخصات في التجريد عنها، (الثاني): من الأمرين اللذين ذكروهما في معنى الكلية ( أن المعلوم بها أي بالصورة العقلية (أمر كلي)، فإذا وصف الصورة بالكلية كان مجازاً على معنى أنها صورة كلي ما علم بها ( وهذا ) الأمر الثاني ( لا يليق بمن يرى العلم غير الصورة الذهنية ) المساوية في الماهية للمعلومات، بل يراه أنه صور ذهنية مخالفة لها في الماهية وتوضيح الكلام أنَّ القائلين بالصور فرقتان : فرقة تدعي أن تلك الصور مساوية في الماهية للأمور المعلومة بها، بل الصور هي ماهيات المعلومات من حيث أنها حاصلة في النفس، فيكون العلم والمعلوم متحدين بالذات مختلفين بالاعتبار كما مر وعلى قول هؤلاء يكون للأشياء وجودان وجود خارجي ووجود ذهني، وتكون الكلية عارضة للصور العقلية حقيقة، لأنها ماهيات المعلومات المحمولة على أفرادها وفرقة تزعم أن الصور العقلية مثل وأشباح للأمور المعلومة بها مخالفة لها في الماهية وعلى قولهم لا يكون للأشياء وجود ذهني بحسب الحقيقة بل بحسب المجاز والتأويل كان يقال مثلاً : النار موجودة في الذهن، ويراد أنَّه يوجد فيه شبح له نسبة مخصوصة إلى ماهية النار بسببها كان ذلك الشبح علماً بالنار لا بغيرها من الماهيات وكنا قد أشرنا إلى ذلك فيما سبق، وكذا على قولهم : لا تكون الكلية عارضة للصور العقلية حقيقة؛ لأن تلك المثل والأشباح ليست محمولة على أفراد المعلومات بتلك الصور، بل المحمول عليها ماهيتها المعلومة بها، فأشار المصنف إلى أنَّ القول بأنَّ الصورة العقلية ليست كلية، إنما الكلي هو المعلوم بها يليق بمذهب هؤلاء لا بمذهب الفرقة الأولى إذ المعلوم والعلم عندهم متحدان ذاتاً. فقوله : يرى العلم غير الصور الذهنية أراد به ما ذكرناه من أنه يرى العلم غير الصور الذهنية المساوية للمعلومات في الماهية، بل يراه صوراً ذهنية مخالفة في الماهية لما علم بها، فمحصول كلامه : يليق بمن يرى المعلوم غير الصور الذهنية، ولو صرّح بهذه العبارة لانتظم أول الكلام مع آخره الذي سيأتي بلا حاجة إلى تأويل كما يشهد به كل فطرة سليمة .
The second type - sixth section: The statement of the philosophers about the intelligible forms. Rather, universality (al-kulliyya) is an expression of correspondence (al-mutabaqa) absolutely, rather it is a correspondence of a shadowy essence that has no rooted existence in existence to that which it is a shadow for. And know that what was mentioned in depicting the correspondence which is the meaning of universality, appears only in the universals that are real types (anwa’ haqiqiyya). So if its parts are intended in the rest of the universals, they are measured to their portions which are their conceptual individuals, for they are real types in relation to them, or what is other than the common meaning among their individuals is made like particularizers in abstraction from them. The second of the two matters they mentioned in the meaning of universality is that the known thing (al-ma’lum) by it, i.e., by the intelligible form, is a universal matter (amr kulli). So if the form is described as universal, it is metaphorical in the sense that it is a form of a universal known by it. And this second matter is not befitting one who holds that knowledge is other than the mental forms that are equal in essence to the known things, rather he holds that it is mental forms that are different in essence from them. And clarifying the statement: Indeed the proponents of forms are two factions: a faction that claims that those forms are equal in essence to the things known by them, rather the forms are the essences of the known things inasmuch as they are obtained in the soul, so knowledge and the known are united in essence and different in consideration, as has passed. And according to these, things have two existences: external existence and mental existence, and universality genuinely attaches to the intelligible forms, because they are the essences of the known things predicated on their individuals. And a faction claims that the intelligible forms are like images and phantoms of the things known by them, different from them in essence. And according to them, things have no mental existence in reality, but rather metaphorically and by interpretation. For example, it is said: 'Fire exists in the mind,' intending that there exists in it a phantom having a specific relation to the essence of fire, due to which that phantom becomes knowledge of fire and not of other essences. And we have indicated that previously. Likewise, according to them, universality does not genuinely attach to the intelligible forms, because those images and phantoms are not predicated on the individuals of the things known by those forms, but rather what is predicated on them is their essence known by them. So the author (al-musannif) indicated that the statement that the intelligible form is not universal, rather the universal is the known thing by it, is befitting the doctrine of these (latter) and not the doctrine of the first faction, since the known and knowledge are united in essence. So his statement: 'He holds that knowledge is other than the mental forms' intends what we mentioned, that he holds that knowledge is other than the mental forms that are equal to the known things in essence, rather he holds that it is mental forms different in essence from what is known by them. So the summary of his statement is: It is befitting one who holds that the known is other than the mental forms. And if he had explicitly stated this expression, the beginning of his speech would be connected with its end that will come, without need for interpretation, as every sound innate disposition attests.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is a definition of universality (kulliyya) and an exposition of two competing factions among the proponents of mental forms. It begins by defining kulliyya as a correspondence (mutabaqa) that is a shadowy essence lacking rooted existence, applying primarily to real types (anwa’ haqiqiyya). For other universals, universality is ascribed by analogy to their conceptual individuals. Then it introduces the second matter: that the thing known through the intelligible form is the universal, not the form itself. This view is only consistent with one of the two factions: those who hold that mental forms are different in essence from known objects. The passage then details both factions: the first holds forms are identical in essence to known things, so universality genuinely attaches to the forms; the second holds forms are mere likenesses, so universality attaches only to the known objects. The author (al-musannif) notes that the second point (that the known is the universal) fits the second faction. The explanation then ties the second faction's view to metaphorical mental existence and shows that the author's wording must be interpreted to mean that the known (not knowledge) is other than the forms, connecting the beginning and end of the text.

Key Terms
كلية (kulliyya)
مطابقة (mutabaqa)
ذات مثالية (dhat mithaliyya)
نوع حقيقي (naw' haqiqi)
أفراد اعتبارية (afrad i'tibariyya)

Checkpoint
According to al-Sanusi's definition, universality is primarily a correspondence with what kind of existence?
A) External, real existence
B) A shadowy, non-rooted existence
C) Absolute existence
D) Conceptual existence only for some universals

Key Takeaway
Universality is defined as a correspondence between a shadowy mental essence and what it represents, not a real attribute of external objects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You hold certain universals—like 'justice' or 'truth'—as sacred ideals. This lesson says they are shadows with no rooted existence. If that is true, what are you actually worshipping? How would your life change if you treated these ideals as tools, not realities?
?
Think of one concept you use daily to judge others, such as 'intelligence' or 'piety.' Is that concept a shadowy essence in your mind that corresponds to something real, or have you mistaken the shadow for the substance? What would it take to see clearly?
?
The passage says universality is a correspondence with a shadow. When you die, every universal concept you carried will dissolve with your mind. What remains that is not a shadow? What are you investing in that will survive the dissolution of your mental categories?

What Is Kalam?
advanced
8 min
Two Factions on Mental Forms and Universality
Explains the two factions on mental forms and their implications for universality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two factions among proponents of mental forms regarding whether forms are identical to or different from known objects, and how this affects whether universality genuinely attaches to forms.

Core Idea
Whether universality attaches to mental forms hinges on whether those forms equal or differ from the essences of known things.

Al-Sanusi now turns to a key debate: those who accept mental forms are divided into two groups. One group holds that mental forms are identical in essence to the things known, so universality genuinely belongs to these forms. The other group sees forms as mere likenesses, different in essence, so only the known objects are universal, not the forms. This distinction determines how we understand mental existence and the predication of universals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two factions
Read the passage that introduces 'al-qa'ilun bi al-suwar firqatan'. Identify the first faction: they claim forms are equal in essence to known things. The second faction: forms are like images and phantoms. Note the key difference.
The first faction is associated with the mainstream of Islamic philosophy (e.g., Avicenna).
2
Understand the implication for universality
For the first faction, universality genuinely attaches to forms because the forms are the essences predicated on individuals. For the second, it does not, because forms are not predicated; only the known essence is.
This relates to the earlier definition: universality is a correspondence; here it's about what bears that correspondence.
3
Connect to mental existence
The first faction affirms two existences (external and mental) and sees universality as real. The second faction only allows mental existence metaphorically. See how this aligns with their views on forms.
This shows the ontological stakes of the debate.
4
Interpret the author's final remark
Al-Sanusi notes that the second point (the known is universal, not the form) only befits the second faction. The author's wording needs interpretation to avoid contradiction. Understand why.
This is a subtle hermeneutical point about the text.

The second type - sixth section: The statement of the philosophers about the intelligible forms. Rather, universality (al-kulliyya) is an expression of correspondence (al-mutabaqa) absolutely, rather it is a correspondence of a shadowy essence that has no rooted existence in existence to that which it is a shadow for. And this second matter is not befitting one who holds that knowledge is other than the mental forms that are equal in essence to the known things, rather he holds that it is mental forms that are different in essence from them. Commentary
This passage is a definition of universality (kulliyya) and an exposition of two competing factions among the proponents of mental forms. It begins by defining kulliyya as a correspondence (mutabaqa) that is a shadowy essence lacking rooted existence, applying primarily to real types (anwa’ haqiqiyya). For other universals, universality is ascribed by analogy to their conceptual individuals. Then it introduces the second matter: that the thing known through the intelligible form is the universal, not the form itself. This view is only consistent with one of the two factions: those who hold that mental forms are different in essence from known objects. The passage then details both factions: the first holds forms are identical in essence to known things, so universality genuinely attaches to the forms; the second holds forms are mere likenesses, so universality attaches only to the known objects. The author (al-musannif) notes that the second point (that the known is the universal) fits the second faction. The explanation then ties the second faction's view to metaphorical mental existence and shows that the author's wording must be interpreted to mean that the known (not knowledge) is other than the forms, connecting the beginning and end of the text.

Key Terms
صورة عقلية (sura 'aqliyya)
وجود ذهني (wujud dhihni)
مثل وأشباح (muthul wa ashbah)
كلية (kulliyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that all proponents of mental forms hold that forms are identical to known objects.
Correction: There are two factions: one holds identity, the other holds that forms are mere likenesses, differing in essence.

Checkpoint
According to the first faction, why does universality genuinely attach to the intelligible forms?
A) Because the forms are shadowy likenesses.
B) Because the forms are the essences of the known things predicated on individuals.
C) Because mental existence is metaphorical.
D) Because the forms are distinct from the known objects.

Key Takeaway
Whether universality genuinely attaches to mental forms depends on whether those forms are regarded as identical to the essences of things or merely as phantoms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your knowledge of Allah’s attributes, do you assume your mental concepts capture His essence directly, or do you suspect they are mere phantoms? Which faction’s view feels more true to your experience—and what does that imply about the certainty of your worship?
?
According to the second faction, universal judgments reside not in your mental forms but in the known essences themselves. Think about a universal you state with confidence (e.g., 'All prophets are truthful'). Is your conviction based on the form in your mind or on the reality you believe? If your mental form is only a phantom, what bridges the gap to the truth?

Detailed vs. Summary Knowledge
Al-Jurjani defines detailed and summary knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between detailed and summary knowledge and how summary knowledge is still genuine knowledge.

Core Idea
Summary knowledge is not ignorance; it is a compressed grasp of the whole at once.

In this passage, al-Jurjani introduces a fundamental classification of knowledge into two types: detailed (tafsīlī) and summary (ijmālī). He defines each with an example, showing that summary knowledge is not a lack of knowledge but a different mode of knowing. Understanding this distinction is essential for grasping how scholars conceive of theological certainty.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic carefully, then the translation. Notice how the author defines detailed knowledge first, then summary.
The passage is from the seventh purpose of al-Mawaqif.
2
Identify the two types and their definitions
Find where detailed knowledge is defined as observing parts one after another, and summary knowledge as bringing the answer to mind at once.
3
Analyze the example of the questioned person
Notice the example: someone who knows a problem and when asked, brings the answer to mind instantly. The author argues this involves conceiving the answer and knowing one's ability, which implies knowledge of the answer's reality.
This refutes the idea that summary knowledge is merely potential.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد السابع : ينقسم العلم إلى أجزاء ومراتب ( العلم ينقسم إلى تفصيلي: وهو أن ينظر إلى أجزائه ومراتبه) أي أجزاء المعلوم ومراتبه بحسب أجزائه بأن يلاحظها واحداً بعد واحد ( وإلى إجمالي كمن يعلم مسألة فيسأل عنها فإنه يحضر الجواب) الذي هو تلك المسألة بأسرها (في ذهنه دفعة واحدة (وهو) أي ذلك الشخص المسؤول (متصور) في ذلك الزمان ( للجواب ) لأنه ( عالم ) حينئذ ( بأنه قادر عليه ولا شك أن علمه باقتداره على الجواب يتضمن علمه بحقيقة ذلك الجواب لأن العلم بالإضافة متوقف على العلم بكلا
The seventh purpose: Knowledge is divided into parts and levels. (Knowledge is divided into detailed [tafṣīlī]: it is to look at its parts and levels) i.e., the parts of the known and its levels according to its parts, by observing them one after another; (and into summary [ijmālī] like one who knows a problem and is asked about it, then he brings to mind the answer) which is that entire problem (in his mind at once; (and he) i.e., that questioned person (is conceiving) at that time (the answer) because he (is knowing) then (that he is capable of it; and there is no doubt that his knowledge of his ability to answer includes his knowledge of the reality of that answer, because knowledge of a relation depends on knowledge of both...
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of knowledge into two types: detailed (tafṣīlī) and summary (ijmālī), from the seventh purpose. The author begins by stating that knowledge is divided into parts and levels. Detailed knowledge is defined as looking at the parts of the known and its levels, observing them one after another. This implies a sequential, analytical grasp. Summary knowledge is exemplified by a person who knows a problem and, when asked about it, brings the entire answer to mind at once. The author notes that such a person is conceiving the answer because they know that they are capable of it. Moreover, the knowledge of one's ability to answer necessarily includes knowledge of the reality of that answer, since knowledge of a relation (i.e., the relation of ability to the answer) depends on knowledge of both relata. This clarifies that summary knowledge is not mere potentiality but actual knowledge of the content, albeit in a compressed form.

Key Terms
العلم التفصيلي
العلم الإجمالي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Summary knowledge is not real knowledge; it's just a potential or vague awareness.
Correction: The passage shows that summary knowledge is genuine because the person knows their ability to answer, which entails knowledge of the answer's reality.

Checkpoint
What is the key difference between detailed and summary knowledge in this passage?
A) Detailed is certain, while summary is probable.
B) Detailed considers parts sequentially, while summary grasps the whole at once.
C) Detailed is for beginners, while summary is for experts.
D) Detailed is mental, while summary is external.

Key Takeaway
Detailed knowledge parses parts sequentially; summary knowledge grasps the whole at once, and both are genuine forms of knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel you only have a 'general' understanding of an Islamic ruling, do you undervalue that summary knowledge as if it were ignorance? How can you instead recognize it as a real, Allah-given knowledge that already contains the essence of the answer?
?
Recall a moment when someone asked you about a religious matter and you hesitated because you couldn't recall the details instantly. Did you trust that you actually knew the answer in summary form? Will you now, based on this passage, train yourself to confidently say 'I know it, let me bring it to mind'?
?
The passage teaches that knowing you are capable of answering includes knowing the actual answer. Is there an area of deen where you doubt your capability? How can this truth remove that doubt and compel you to act on what you already know?

The Three States of Knowledge and the Simile of Seeing a Flock
Explains the three states of knowledge and illustrates with a simile of seeing a flock.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three psychological states related to knowing an answer and how a simile illustrates the difference between summary and detailed knowledge.

Core Idea
Knowledge moves from pure potential to actual awareness to detailed understanding.

In this passage, the author explains the three distinct psychological states a person experiences when asked a question: the initial ignorance, the sudden awareness that follows, and the detailed elaboration. He then provides a striking simile of seeing a flock to make the distinction self-evident. This lesson unpacks these states and the simile, showing how they form a foundation for Islamic epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation carefully. Let the author's description of the three states and the simile settle in your mind.
Pay attention to the sequence: ignorance, immediate awareness, detailed knowledge.
2
Identify the three states
Locate in the text the descriptions of the three states: (1) before the question, (2) immediately after the question, (3) during detailed observation. Note how the author distinguishes each.
The state after the question is neither ignorance nor full detail; it is a middle state of actual awareness.
3
Reflect on the simile
Consider the analogy of seeing a flock of sheep. How does the first glance correspond to summary knowledge? How does focusing on each sheep correspond to detailed knowledge? The author says the difference is self-evident.
Think about how this applies to inner sight (the intellect) as opposed to outer sight.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
طَرَفَيْهَا ( ثُمَّ يَأْخُذُ فِي تَقْرِيرِهِ أَيْ تَقْرِيرِ الْجَوَابِ ) فَيُلَاحِظُ تَفْصِيلَهُ ) بِمُلَاحَظَةِ أَجْزَائِهِ وَاحِدًا بَعْدَ وَاحِدٍ فَفِي ذِهْنِهِ حَالَ مَا سُئِلَ (أَمْرٌ بَسِيطٌ وَهُوَ مَبْدَأُ التَّفَاصِيلِ) الْحَاصِلَةِ فِي ثَانِي الْحَالِ ) وَالتَّفَرُّقَةُ بَيْنَ تِلْكَ الْحَالَةِ ) الْحَاصِلَةِ دَفْعَةً عَقِيبَ السُّؤَالِ ( وَبَيْنَ حَالَةِ الْجَهْلِ ) الثَّابِتَةِ قَبْلَ السُّؤَالِ ) وَمُلَاحَظَةُ التَّفْصِيلِ) الْمُتَفَرِّعَةُ عَلَى التَّقْرِيرِ ( ضَرُورِيَّةٌ ) وِجْدَانِيَّةٌ إِذْ فِي حَالَةِ الْجَهْلِ الْمُسَمَّاةِ عَقْلًا بِالْفِعْلِ لَيْسَ إِدْرَاكُ الْجَوَابِ حَاصِلًا بِالْفِعْلِ، بَلِ النَّفْسُ فِي تِلْكَ الْحَالَةِ تَقْوَى عَلَى اسْتِحْضَارِهِ وَتَفْصِيلِهِ بِلَا تَجَشُّمِ كَسْبٍ جَدِيدٍ، فَهُنَاكَ قُوَّةٌ مَحْضَةٌ وَفِي الْحَالَةِ الْحَاصِلَةِ عَقِيبَ السُّؤَالِ قَدْ حَصَلَ بِالْفِعْلِ شُعُورٌ وَعِلْمٌ مَا بِالْجَوَابِ لَمْ يَكُنْ حَاصِلًا قَبْلَهُ وَفِي الْحَالَةِ التَّفْصِيلِيَّةِ صَارَتِ الْأَجْزَاءُ مَلْحُوظَةً قَصْدًا، وَلَمْ يَكُنْ ذَلِكَ حَاصِلًا فِي شَيْءٍ مِنَ الْحَالَتَيْنِ السَّابِقَتَيْنِ ( وَشُبِّهَ ذَلِكَ بِمَنْ يَرَى نَعَمًا) كَثِيرًا ( تَارَةً دَفْعَةً فَإِنَّهُ يَرَى فِي هَذِهِ الْحَالَةِ ( جَمِيعَ أَجْزَائِهِ ) أَيْ أَجْزَاءَ ذَلِكَ النَّعَمِ ( ضَرُورَةً وَتَارَةً بِأَنْ يُحَدِّقَ الْبَصَرَ نَحْوَ وَاحِدٍ وَاحِدٍ فَيُمَيِّزَهُ أَيِ النَّعَمَ وَيُفَصِّلَ أَجْزَاءَهُ بَعْضَهَا عَنْ بَعْضٍ، فَالرُّؤْيَةُ الْأُولَى رُؤْيَةٌ إِجْمَالِيَّةٌ، وَالثَّانِيَةُ رُؤْيَةٌ تَفْصِيلِيَّةٌ وَالْفَرْقُ بَيْنَهُمَا مَعْلُومٌ بِالْوِجْدَانِ فَقِسْ حَالَةَ الْبَصِيرَةِ بِالنِّسْبَةِ إِلَى مَدْرَكَاتِهَا عَلَى حَالَةِ الْبَصَرِ بِالْقِيَاسِ إِلَى مَدْرَكَاتِهِ فِي ثُبُوتِ مِثْلِ هَاتَيْنِ الْحَالَتَيْنِ فِيهَا أَيْضًا )
Its two sides (then he proceeds to its determination i.e. determination of the answer) so he observes its detail by observing its parts one after another. Thus in his mind at the moment he was asked (there is a simple matter which is the principle of the details) occurring in the second moment (and the distinction between that state) occurring instantly after the question (and the state of ignorance) established before the question (and the observation of the detail) branching from the determination (is necessary) and self-evident. For in the state of ignorance called potentially actual intellect, the perception of the answer is not actually present; rather the soul in that state is able to bring it to mind and detail it without undertaking new acquisition. So there is pure potentiality. In the state occurring after the question, there has actually occurred a feeling and some knowledge of the answer that was not present before it. In the detailed state the parts come to be intentionally observed, and that was not present in either of the two previous states. (And that is likened to one who sees) many camels/sheep (sometimes all at once, for he sees in this state) all its parts i.e. the parts of that flock (necessarily, and sometimes by fixing the gaze towards one by one, so he distinguishes it i.e. the flock and differentiates its parts from one another. So the first vision is summary vision, and the second is detailed vision, and the difference between them is known by direct experience. So compare the state of inner sight in relation to its objects to the state of outer sight in relation to its objects in the existence of such two states in it also.)
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage is a definition and illustration of the three psychological states related to knowledge of an answer. It begins by describing the process: when asked a question, the mind initially has a simple, undetailed awareness (the principle of the details). Then the author distinguishes three states: (1) the state of ignorance (before the question) where the answer is not present at all, only a pure potential to acquire it; (2) the state immediately after the question, where a feeling or some knowledge of the answer occurs — this is neither ignorance nor full detail, but a middle state; (3) the detailed state, where the parts of the answer are observed one by one intentionally. The author emphasizes that the distinction between these states is self-evident and necessary. Then a simile is provided: seeing a large flock of animals. In the first glance, one sees the whole flock summarily; then by focusing on each animal individually, one achieves a detailed vision. The analogy is then extended to inner sight (the intellect) compared to outer sight (the eye). This passage is foundational for understanding the concept of 'summary knowledge' (al-ʿilm al-ijmālī) in Islamic epistemology.

علم تفصيلي
حالة الجهل
قوة محضة
رؤية إجمالية
رؤية تفصيلية
وجدان

Correction: The author insists it is actual knowledge, not pure potential.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The distinction between ignorance, immediate awareness, and detailed knowledge is self-evident, as illustrated by seeing a flock summarily and then in detail.

Al-Razi's Objection: No Single Image for Multiple Things
Al-Razi denies summary knowledge and reinterprets it as mere coexistence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam al-Razi argued that summary knowledge is impossible and how he accounted for simultaneous and successive mental images.

Core Idea
One image cannot be the image of many different realities.

Imam al-Razi launches a powerful objection against the concept of summary knowledge. He argues that a single mental image cannot correspond to multiple distinct things, because if it did, it would have to share the quiddities of all of them, making it not really one image. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and his treatment of cases where multiple images occur together or in sequence.

Guided Path
1
Al-Razi's core argument
Read how al-Razi argues that a single image cannot match multiple distinct objects. Understand the logic: if one image equaled many quiddities, it would itself have many realities.
Focus on the phrase 'صورة واحدة مطابقة لأمور مختلفة'.
2
The necessity of multiple images
Notice his conclusion that each known thing must have its own separate image. This leads to the redefinition of detailed knowledge.
Detailed knowledge is simply having multiple images.
3
Simultaneous and successive images
Al-Razi allows that multiple images can occur either all at once (as in conceiving a whole) or one after another. This is his concession to the opponent's claim.
He denies that this constitutes a distinct intermediate state.
4
Reinterpreting summary knowledge
See how al-Razi reduces the so-called 'summary knowledge' to a mere accidental coexistence of multiple detailed knowledges. Both states are actually detailed in reality.
The dispute is only about naming, not about the nature of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الإمام الرازي : ) في إنكار العلم الإجمالي ( يمتنع حصول صورة واحدة مطابقة لأمور مختلفة لأن الصورة الواحدة لو طابقت أموراً مختلفة لكانت مساوية في الماهية لتلك الأمور المختلفة فيكون لتلك الصورة حقائق مختلفة فلا تكون صورة واحدة (بل) يجب أن يكون ( لكل واحد ) من الأمور المتكثرة ( صورة ) على حدة ولا معنى للعلم التفصيلي إلا ذلك ) أعني أن يكون للمعلومات المتكثرة صور متعددة بحسبها فينكشف كل معلوم منها بصورته ويمتاز عما عداه ( نعم إنه قد تحصل الصور) المتعددة لأمور متكثرة كأجزاء المركب ) تارة (دفعة ( كما إذا تصور حقيقة المركب من حيث هو وتارة مترتبة في الزمان كما إذا تصور أجزاؤه واحداً بعد واحد ( فإن أرادوا بما ذكروه من العلم الإجمالي والتفصيلي ) ذلك ) الذي ذكرناه من حصول الصورة تارة دفعة وأخرى مترتبة ( فلا نزاع فيه إلا أن الإجمالي بهذا المعنى لا يكون حالة متوسطة بين القوة المحضة التي هي حالة الجهل وبين العقل المحض الذي هو حالة التفصيل لأن حاصله راجع إلى أن العلوم، قد تجتمع في زمان واحد وقد لا تجتمع بل تتعاقب وبذلك لا يختلف حال العلم بالقياس إلى المعلوم فكلتا الحالتين علم تفصيلي بحسب الحقيقة والخلاف في التسمية باعتبار الاجتماع العارض للمعلوم لا باعتبار اختلافها مقيسة إلى المعلومات، قال : وأما ما قالوه من أنه عقيب السؤال عالم بالجواب إجمالاً لا تفصيلاً لترتبه على التقرير فمردود بأن لذلك الجواب حقيقة وماهية وله لازم وهو أنه شيء يصلح جواباً لذلك السؤال والمعلوم عقيب السؤال هو ذلكم اللازم، وهو معلوم بالتفصيل، وأما الحقيقة فهي مجهولة في تلك الحالة، ونظير ذلك أنا إذا عرفنا النفس من حيث أنها شيء يحرك البدن فإن لازمها، أعني كونها محركة معلومة تفصيلا وحقيقتها مجهولة إلى أن تعرف بطريق آخر فبطل ما قالوه، وظهر أيضاً أنَّ العلم الواحد لا يكون علماً بمعلومات كثيرة أقول : ومن إنكاره العلم الإجمالي نشأ إنكاره للاكتساب في التصورات والجواب : أنه إذا علم المركب
Imam al-Razi said: (In denial of summary knowledge) it is impossible for a single image to correspond to different things, because if a single image corresponded to different things, it would be equal in quiddity to those different things, so that image would have different realities and thus would not be a single image. Rather, it must be that for each of the multiple things there is an image separately, and the meaning of detailed knowledge is nothing but that: that for multiple knowns there are multiple images corresponding to them, so each known is disclosed by its image and is distinguished from what is other than it. Yes, it is possible that the multiple images of multiple things, like the parts of a composite, may occur (sometimes) all at once, as when one conceives the reality of the composite insofar as it is a whole, and sometimes successively in time, as when one conceives its parts one by one. If what they mention of summary and detailed knowledge refers to that — namely the occurrence of the image sometimes at once and sometimes successively — then there is no dispute, except that summary knowledge in this sense is not an intermediate state between pure potentiality which is the state of ignorance and pure actuality which is the state of detail, because its content reduces to the fact that knowledges may coexist in one time or may not coexist but rather follow one another, and by that the state of knowledge in relation to the known does not differ. So both states are detailed knowledge in reality, and the dispute is only over naming, considering the accidental coexistence of the known, not considering their difference in relation to the knowns. He said: As for their saying that after the question one knows the answer summarily not in detail because of its being based on the determination, it is refuted by the fact that that answer has a reality and quiddity, and it has a necessary property, namely that it is something suitable as an answer to that question. What is known after the question is that necessary property, and it is known in detail, while the reality is unknown in that state. An analogy for that is when we know the soul insofar as it is something that moves the body: its necessary property, namely its being a mover, is known in detail, while its reality is unknown until it is known by another method. So what they said is invalid, and it also became clear that a single knowledge cannot be knowledge of many things. I say: And from his denial of summary knowledge arose his denial of acquisition in conceptualizations, and the answer: when one knows the composite...
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage presents an objection by Imam al-Razi against the concept of summary knowledge (al-ʿilm al-ijmālī) and the subsequent refutation by the commentator (likely al-Jurjānī or the author of the sharḥ). The passage begins by stating al-Razi's core argument: a single mental image cannot correspond to multiple distinct realities, because if it did, it would have to share the quiddities of all of them, making it multiple images rather than one. Therefore, each known object must have its own separate image; detailed knowledge is simply having multiple images each corresponding to its object. The refutation first concedes that images may occur simultaneously or successively, but argues that this does not constitute a distinct intermediate state between ignorance and full detail. Instead, both are detailed knowledge; the only difference is whether the multiple images are present together or in sequence. The main response then is a nuanced one: when a question is asked, what is known about the answer is not its essence/reality (al-ḥaqīqa) but its necessary property (lāzim) — that it is the answer to that question. That necessary property is known in detail; the essence remains unknown until later. This is compared to knowing the soul as a mover without knowing its reality. Hence, summary knowledge in the original sense (as an intermediate state) is reinterpreted as detailed knowledge of a property. The passage ends with an observation that al-Razi's denial of summary knowledge leads him to deny acquisition (iktisāb) in conceptualizations, and a brief rebuttal that when one knows the composite... (the text cuts off but the idea is that summary knowledge of the composite is possible).

The Refutation: Knowledge of the Necessary Property, Not the Essence
Refutation of al-Razi using the distinction between essence and necessary property.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the refutation defends summary knowledge by distinguishing between knowledge of a thing's essence and knowledge of its necessary property, and how this resolves al-Razi's objection.

Core Idea
Summary knowledge is precise knowledge of a necessary property, not a vague grasp of the essence.

After presenting al-Razi's objection, the commentator refutes it by introducing a crucial distinction. When a question is asked, what we know about the answer is not its full reality but its necessary property — that it is suitable as an answer. This knowledge is detailed, not summary. The same applies to knowing the soul as a mover. This lesson explores that refutation and its implications for the concept of acquisition (iktisāb) in conceptualizations.

Guided Path
1
The claim about knowledge after a question
Identify the opponent's view that after a question one knows the answer summary. Al-Razi refutes it by analyzing what exactly is known.
Pay attention to the terms 'حقيقة وماهية' and 'لازم'.
2
Distinguishing essence from necessary property
The refutation says the answer has a reality (essence) and a necessary property. The known is the necessary property (the suitability as answer), and that is known in detail.
The unknown is the essence, which is not yet known.
3
Analogy with knowing the soul
The commentator gives an analogy: we know the soul as a mover (property) but not its reality. This illustrates the same distinction.
This shows the pattern is general.
4
Implication for acquisition in conceptualizations
Al-Razi's denial of summary knowledge leads him to deny acquisition in conceptualizations. The refutation hints that summary knowledge of a composite is possible.
The text ends mid-sentence; the idea is that when one knows the composite, one has a kind of summary knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الإمام الرازي : ) في إنكار العلم الإجمالي ( يمتنع حصول صورة واحدة مطابقة لأمور مختلفة لأن الصورة الواحدة لو طابقت أموراً مختلفة لكانت مساوية في الماهية لتلك الأمور المختلفة فيكون لتلك الصورة حقائق مختلفة فلا تكون صورة واحدة (بل) يجب أن يكون ( لكل واحد ) من الأمور المتكثرة ( صورة ) على حدة ولا معنى للعلم التفصيلي إلا ذلك ) أعني أن يكون للمعلومات المتكثرة صور متعددة بحسبها فينكشف كل معلوم منها بصورته ويمتاز عما عداه ( نعم إنه قد تحصل الصور) المتعددة لأمور متكثرة كأجزاء المركب ) تارة (دفعة ( كما إذا تصور حقيقة المركب من حيث هو وتارة مترتبة في الزمان كما إذا تصور أجزاؤه واحداً بعد واحد ( فإن أرادوا بما ذكروه من العلم الإجمالي والتفصيلي ) ذلك ) الذي ذكرناه من حصول الصورة تارة دفعة وأخرى مترتبة ( فلا نزاع فيه إلا أن الإجمالي بهذا المعنى لا يكون حالة متوسطة بين القوة المحضة التي هي حالة الجهل وبين العقل المحض الذي هو حالة التفصيل لأن حاصله راجع إلى أن العلوم، قد تجتمع في زمان واحد وقد لا تجتمع بل تتعاقب وبذلك لا يختلف حال العلم بالقياس إلى المعلوم فكلتا الحالتين علم تفصيلي بحسب الحقيقة والخلاف في التسمية باعتبار الاجتماع العارض للمعلوم لا باعتبار اختلافها مقيسة إلى المعلومات، قال : وأما ما قالوه من أنه عقيب السؤال عالم بالجواب إجمالاً لا تفصيلاً لترتبه على التقرير فمردود بأن لذلك الجواب حقيقة وماهية وله لازم وهو أنه شيء يصلح جواباً لذلك السؤال والمعلوم عقيب السؤال هو ذلكم اللازم، وهو معلوم بالتفصيل، وأما الحقيقة فهي مجهولة في تلك الحالة، ونظير ذلك أنا إذا عرفنا النفس من حيث أنها شيء يحرك البدن فإن لازمها، أعني كونها محركة معلومة تفصيلا وحقيقتها مجهولة إلى أن تعرف بطريق آخر فبطل ما قالوه، وظهر أيضاً أنَّ العلم الواحد لا يكون علماً بمعلومات كثيرة أقول : ومن إنكاره العلم الإجمالي نشأ إنكاره للاكتساب في التصورات والجواب : أنه إذا علم المركب
Imam al-Razi said: (In denial of summary knowledge) it is impossible for a single image to correspond to different things, because if a single image corresponded to different things, it would be equal in quiddity to those different things, so that image would have different realities and thus would not be a single image. Rather, it must be that for each of the multiple things there is an image separately, and the meaning of detailed knowledge is nothing but that: that for multiple knowns there are multiple images corresponding to them, so each known is disclosed by its image and is distinguished from what is other than it. Yes, it is possible that the multiple images of multiple things, like the parts of a composite, may occur (sometimes) all at once, as when one conceives the reality of the composite insofar as it is a whole, and sometimes successively in time, as when one conceives its parts one by one. If what they mention of summary and detailed knowledge refers to that — namely the occurrence of the image sometimes at once and sometimes successively — then there is no dispute, except that summary knowledge in this sense is not an intermediate state between pure potentiality which is the state of ignorance and pure actuality which is the state of detail, because its content reduces to the fact that knowledges may coexist in one time or may not coexist but rather follow one another, and by that the state of knowledge in relation to the known does not differ. So both states are detailed knowledge in reality, and the dispute is only over naming, considering the accidental coexistence of the known, not considering their difference in relation to the knowns. He said: As for their saying that after the question one knows the answer summarily not in detail because of its being based on the determination, it is refuted by the fact that that answer has a reality and quiddity, and it has a necessary property, namely that it is something suitable as an answer to that question. What is known after the question is that necessary property, and it is known in detail, while the reality is unknown in that state. An analogy for that is when we know the soul insofar as it is something that moves the body: its necessary property, namely its being a mover, is known in detail, while its reality is unknown until it is known by another method. So what they said is invalid, and it also became clear that a single knowledge cannot be knowledge of many things. I say: And from his denial of summary knowledge arose his denial of acquisition in conceptualizations, and the answer: when one knows the composite...
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage presents an objection by Imam al-Razi against the concept of summary knowledge (al-ʿilm al-ijmālī) and the subsequent refutation by the commentator (likely al-Jurjānī or the author of the sharḥ). The passage begins by stating al-Razi's core argument: a single mental image cannot correspond to multiple distinct realities, because if it did, it would have to share the quiddities of all of them, making it multiple images rather than one. Therefore, each known object must have its own separate image; detailed knowledge is simply having multiple images each corresponding to its object. The refutation first concedes that images may occur simultaneously or successively, but argues that this does not constitute a distinct intermediate state between ignorance and full detail. Instead, both are detailed knowledge; the only difference is whether the multiple images are present together or in sequence. The main response then is a nuanced one: when a question is asked, what is known about the answer is not its essence/reality (al-ḥaqīqa) but its necessary property (lāzim) — that it is the answer to that question. That necessary property is known in detail; the essence remains unknown until later. This is compared to knowing the soul as a mover without knowing its reality. Hence, summary knowledge in the original sense (as an intermediate state) is reinterpreted as detailed knowledge of a property. The passage ends with an observation that al-Razi's denial of summary knowledge leads him to deny acquisition (iktisāb) in conceptualizations, and a brief rebuttal that when one knows the composite... (the text cuts off but the idea is that summary knowledge of the composite is possible).

Key Terms
لازم
حقيقة
ماهية
اكتساب في التصورات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that summary knowledge after a question is vague or incomplete in a way that is not genuine knowledge.
Correction: The refutation shows it is genuine detailed knowledge of the necessary property, not a vague grasp.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, what is known immediately after a question?
A) The full essence of the answer.
B) The necessary property of the answer, in detail.
C) A vague image of possible answers.
D) Nothing yet; knowledge comes later.

Key Takeaway
Summary knowledge is not a vague or intermediate state; it is detailed knowledge of the necessary property of a thing while its essence remains unknown.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own soul, do you settle for knowing its necessary property (like 'it moves my body') without seeking its true reality? What would it take to move from property knowledge to essence knowledge, and are you willing to do that?
?

Summary and Detailed Knowledge: No Middle State
Defines summary and detailed knowledge and denies any intermediate state between ignorance and intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of summary and detailed knowledge, and why summary knowledge is not a separate middle state between ignorance and full intellect.

Core Idea
Summary knowledge is still detailed knowledge, just with simultaneous forms.

This lesson examines a fundamental distinction in Islamic epistemology: the difference between summary and detailed knowledge. The author clarifies that what is called 'summary' knowledge is not a weaker form of knowing but simply a grouping of detailed forms occurring together. The key claim is that there is no third state between ignorance and full intellect.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the author's precise definitions.
The passage uses technical terms; keep them in mind.
2
Identify the definition of detailed knowledge
Find where the author defines detailed knowledge. Notice it requires distinct forms for each known thing, so each is disclosed separately.
3
Understand the explanation of summary knowledge
The author explains that summary knowledge is when those forms are obtained at once (simultaneously) rather than sequentially. This is the only difference.
This is a key clarification.
4
Grasp the denial of a middle state
Focus on why summary knowledge is not an intermediate state. The author argues both simultaneous and sequential are actually detailed knowledge; the label 'summary' is merely due to accidental assembly.
This refutes a common misunderstanding.
5
Reflect on the doctrinal significance
Consider why this distinction matters: it prevents treating summary knowledge as a distinct epistemic category weaker than detailed knowledge.

(For each one) of the multiple things (a form) separately, and detailed knowledge (al-ʿilm at-tafṣīlī) means nothing but that, i.e. that for multiple known things there are multiple forms corresponding to them, so each known thing is disclosed by its form and distinguished from what is other than it. (Yes, it is possible that the multiple forms) for multiple things, like parts of a composite, (sometimes at once) as when one imagines the reality of the composite insofar as it is composite, and sometimes sequentially in time as when one imagines its parts one after another. Commentary
This passage defines two types of knowledge: detailed knowledge (tafṣīlī) and summary knowledge (ijmālī). The author states that detailed knowledge fundamentally means having distinct mental forms for each known thing, so that each is disclosed separately. Summary knowledge, as commonly understood, is simply when multiple forms are obtained at once (e.g., knowing a composite in its entirety) versus sequentially (detailed). The key point is that the so-called 'summary knowledge' is not a third intermediate state between ignorance (pure potentiality) and detailed intellect. Rather, it is still detailed knowledge in reality; the only difference is whether multiple known items are present simultaneously or not. Therefore, there is no genuine middle state. The commentator here affirms that both acute and summary are in fact detailed, and the label 'summary' is merely due to the accidental coexistence of the items in one moment of knowledge.

Two Ranks of Knowledge: Total and Detailed
Defines the two ranks of knowledge in relation to its object.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between total (ijmālī) and detailed (tafsīlī) knowledge and how they relate to the known object.

Core Idea
Knowledge of a composite can be either implicit (total) or explicit (detailed).

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif explains a fundamental epistemological concept: the two ranks of knowledge. By understanding how the mind relates to a composite object—either grasping it as a whole or analyzing its parts—we see that knowledge is not uniform. The author uses the example of a composite to illustrate how the same object can be known in two ways, one weaker and one stronger.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how the author describes the mind's focus on the composite versus its parts.
Pay attention to the words 'إجمالي' and 'تفصيلي'.
2
Identify the two ranks of knowledge
Find where the passage explicitly states 'فللعلم بالقياس إلى معلومه مرتبتان إحداهما إجمالي والأخرى تفصيلي'. Understand that 'total' refers to implicit knowledge of the parts when focusing on the whole, and 'detailed' refers to explicit knowledge when the parts are separated.
3
Understand the mental process
The passage describes how when the composite is known intentionally, its parts are present in the mind but 'stored' and unattended. When the mind turns to them, they become fully disclosed. This shows that the parts were known implicitly all along.
This demonstrates that implicit knowledge is still real knowledge, just not attended to.
4
Connect to the broader discussion
The passage ends with a reference to conceptual vs. assertive knowledge. See how this distinction relates to the two ranks: a thing can be conceptually known (total) but not assertively known (detailed) unless separated.
This connects to the idea of acquisition (kasb) in assents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لم بحقيقته حصل في الذهن صورة واحدة مركبة من صور متعددة بحسب تلك الأجزاء والعقل حينئذ متوجه قصدا إلى ذلك المركب دون أجزائه، فإنها مع حصول صورها في العقل كالمخزون المعرض عنه الذي لا يلتفت إليه فإذا توجه العقل إليها وفصلها صارت مخطرة بالبال ملحوظة قصداً منكشفة بعضها عن بعض انكشافاً تاما، يكن ذلك الانكشاف حاصلا في الحالة الأولى مع حصول صور الأجزاء في الحالتين معا، فظهر أنه قد يتفاوت حال العلم بالقياس إلى المعلوم، وأنه إذا كان المركب معلوما بحقيقته قصدا كان أجزاؤه معلومة حينئذ بلا قصد وإخطار، وإذا فصلت الأجزاء كان العلم بها على وجه أقوى وأكمل من الوجه الأول، فللعلم بالقياس إلى معلومه مرتبتان إحداهما إجمالي والأخرى تفصيلي، كما ذكروه. وقوله : المعلوم عقيب السؤال بين العلم بالشيء بالوجه والعلم بالوجه وإن كان كون الشيء الواحد متصورا بوجه دون وجه، ولذا لم ينكر الاكتساب في التصديقات لوجود النسبة التي يتعلق بها العلم التصوري والعلم التصديقي، فيجوز أن تكون معلومة تصورا مجهولة تصديقا.
When a composite is obtained in the mind in its essence, a single image composed of multiple images according to those parts occurs, and the intellect is then intentionally directed toward that composite without its parts, for they, while their images are present in the mind, are like stored [items] disregarded and not attended to. Then when the intellect turns to them and separates them, they become brought to mind, intentionally noticed, some of them fully disclosed from others. That disclosure was not present in the first state, although the images of the parts were present in both states. So it is apparent that the state of knowledge may vary in relation to the known, and that when the composite is known in its essence intentionally, its parts are then known without intention and bringing to mind, and when the parts are separated, knowledge of them is stronger and more complete than the first manner. Thus knowledge in relation to its known has two ranks: one total and the other detailed, as they mentioned. And His statement: 'The known after the question distinguishes between knowledge of a thing by one aspect and knowledge by another aspect, even though the same thing can be conceived by one aspect and not by another, and therefore acquisition was not denied in assents because of the existence of the relation to which conceptual knowledge and assertive knowledge pertain, so it is permissible for something to be conceptually known but unknown assertively.'
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage defines the two ranks of knowledge in relation to its object. It begins by explaining the case of a composite object: when the composite is known in its essence, the mind has a single image that is composed of multiple images corresponding to the parts, but the mind is focused on the whole, not the parts. The parts are present in the mind but like stored items not attended to. When the mind turns to them and separates them, they become explicitly noticed and fully disclosed. This shows that knowledge can vary: if the composite is known intentionally, its parts are known implicitly without explicit attention; if the parts are separated, knowledge of them is stronger and more complete. Therefore knowledge relative to its known has two ranks: total (ijmālī) and detailed (tafsīlī). The passage then comments on a previous statement about the known after a question, distinguishing between knowledge by one aspect and another, noting that a single thing can be conceived by one aspect but not another, and this allows for conceptual knowledge without assent.

Correction: The passage shows that in total knowledge, the parts are present in the mind and known, but without explicit attention. They are like stored items—still known, just not attended to.

Checkpoint

A) Conceptual and assertive
B) Total and detailed
C) Simple and composite

Key Takeaway
The two ranks of knowledge—total and detailed—describe whether the parts of a composite are known implicitly or explicitly, with detailed knowledge being stronger and more complete.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to know Allah's names and attributes, but is your knowledge total (implicit) or detailed (explicit)? When you recite 'al-Rahman', do you intentionally attend to its meaning, or is it just a stored image in your mind? ?
In your daily life, you often have total knowledge of your duties (e.g., prayer, charity) but neglect to separate and examine them individually. Think of one religious obligation you know in total but have not yet made detailed. When will you turn your intellect to it and bring it to explicit awareness? Like stored items, they are present but disregarded. Stop disregarding.
?
The passage says that when the intellect turns to the parts, they become 'brought to mind, intentionally noticed, fully disclosed'. When was the last time you gave full intentional attention to a single verse of the Quran or a single attribute of Allah? If not now, when? Don't settle for total knowledge; seek the stronger and more complete rank.

The Second Branch: Known and Unknown Are Distinct
Al-Baqillani asserts the known and unknown are distinct, and explains figurative usage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Baqillani's argument that the known and unknown are necessarily distinct, and why the common phrase 'one thing known from one aspect and unknown from another' is figurative.

Core Idea
The known and unknown are never the same thing—they are always distinct objects.

Al-Baqillani tackles a subtle point: can a single thing be both known and unknown? He argues that it's impossible literally, but offers three relational scenarios that make the phrase figuratively acceptable. This lesson unpacks his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Get a feel for the argument before diving into analysis.
Notice the strong opening: 'المعلوم غير المجهول ضرورة'.
2
Identify the main claim
Al-Baqillani's central thesis is that the known and unknown are two distinct things (شيئان متغايران قطعاً). Find the sentence where he states this.
3
Examine the three scenarios
The passage lists three ways the known and unknown can relate: (1) one is an accident of the other, (2) both are accidents of a third, (3) another relation like part-whole. List them and note how each keeps the two distinct.
These show that even when they seem connected, they remain separate.
4
Understand the metaphor
Al-Baqillani says calling these cases 'one thing known from one aspect and unknown from another' is metaphorical (مجاز). Why is it not literal?
Because literally, the known and unknown are different objects, not the same object with two aspects.
5
Reflect on the significance
This argument prevents confusion in theological epistemology: it safeguards that ignorance and knowledge have distinct objects, which is important for discussions about divine attributes and human knowledge.
It also shows how kalam uses precise logical distinctions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرع الثاني : المشهور أنَّ الشيء الواحد قد يكون معلوماً من وجه دون وجه، قال القاضي الباقلاني: المعلوم غير المجهول ضرورة فمتعلق العلم والجهل شيئان متغايران قطعاً وإن كان أحدهما عارضاً للآخر كما إذا علم الإنسان باعتبار ضاحكيته وجهل باعتبار حقيقته أو هما عارضان لثالث كما إذا علم باعتبار ضحكه وجهل باعتبار كتابته أو بينهما تعلق آخر سوى تعلق العروض على أحد الوجهين أي تعلق التعلقات كالجزئية والكلية والاتصال والمجاورة فإن هذه التعلقات لا تقتضي اتحاد المعلوم والمجهول بل تغايرهما والتسمية مجاز يعني أنه إذا كان المعلوم عارضاً للمجهول، أو كانا عارضين لثالث، أو كان بينهما تعلق بوجه آخر وأطلق على هذه الصور أنها من قبيل كون الشيء الواحد معلوماً من وجه، ومجهولاً من وجه آخر، كان هذا الإطلاق من باب التجوز ولا مشاحة ولا منازعة فيه أي في الإطلاق مجازاً فإن بابه مفتوح
The second branch: It is well-known that a single thing may be known from one aspect and unknown from another. Al-Qadi al-Baqillani said: The known is necessarily other than the unknown, so the object of knowledge and the object of ignorance are two things that are absolutely distinct, even if one is an accident of the other—as when a human is known in terms of his laughter and unknown in terms of his essence—or both are accidents of a third—as when he is known in terms of his laughter and unknown in terms of his writing—or there is another relation between them besides that of accidental occurrence on one of the two sides, i.e., relations like partiality, totality, connection, and adjacency, for these relations do not necessitate the union of the known and the unknown but rather their distinctness. And the naming is metaphorical, meaning that if the known is an accident of the unknown, or both are accidents of a third, or there is a relation between them in another way, and these cases are described as 'a single thing being known from one aspect and unknown from another aspect,' this description is a metaphor. There is no dispute or objection to it, i.e., to describing it as metaphorical, for the door of metaphor is open.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage defines the second branch of knowledge: whether one thing can be known from one aspect and unknown from another. Al-Baqillani states that the known and unknown are necessarily distinct—they cannot be the same thing. He lists three possible relational scenarios: (1) one is an accident of the other, (2) both are accidents of a third, or (3) there is a non-accidental relation like part-whole. In all cases, the known and unknown remain distinct objects; they are not the same entity. The phrase 'one thing known from one aspect and unknown from another' is therefore a figurative usage, not literal. The author then notes that figurative language is permissible and no one disputes it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'one thing known from one aspect and unknown from another' means the same entity is both known and unknown at the same time.
Correction: Al-Baqillani clarifies that the known and unknown are always distinct objects; the phrase is metaphorical, not literal.

Checkpoint
According to al-Baqillani, if a person is known for his laughter and unknown for his essence, what is the relationship between the known and the unknown?
A) They are the same thing under different descriptions.
B) The known is an accident of the unknown.
C) They are two distinct things.
D) The unknown is an accident of the known.

Key Takeaway
Al-Baqillani establishes that the known and unknown are always distinct objects; any appearance of unity is metaphorical, not literal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Al-Baqillani says the known and unknown are never the same thing. In what area of your life have you conflated a partial understanding with complete knowledge, and what must you do to humbly acknowledge your ignorance?
?
The door of metaphor is open—but do you use metaphorical language to mask intellectual laziness, avoiding the hard work of precise distinction? Where in your faith do you need to replace vague talk with exact knowledge that changes how you live?
